DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judicial conduct board sought review of an order from the Court of Judicial Discipline (Pennsylvania) that dismissed appellant's charges against appellee, district justice, resulting from two counts of misconduct lodged against appellee. Appellant also sought review of two discovery orders.


OVERVIEW: Disciplinary charges were lodged against appellee, district justice, after incidences of alleged misconduct. The court of judicial discipline dismissed the charges. Appellant, judicial conduct board, sought review of the decision and that of two discovery orders. The cause arose following an investigation that lead to the discovery of marijuana at appellee's residence, and allegations of improprieties with a young girl. Prior to trial, appellee requested and received the identity of the complainant and the minutes of the meeting effectuating the investigation. Upon review, the court held that disclosure of the complainant was moot, but held that a waiver of confidentially by a judicial officer did not automatically entitle him to receive all the confidential information acquired by the board in the course of its investigation. The court of judicial discipline erred in releasing the information to appellee. Further, the court held that the marijuana discovery was a factual finding and was not subject to review by the court. Lastly, the facts revealed appellee did nothing improper with the girl. The court affirmed the court of judicial discipline and dismissed the charges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of judicial discipline and dismissed the charges because the possession of marijuana was a factual finding not reviewable by the appellate court, and the record revealed no impropriety had occurred with the young girl.

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Pennsylvania, at No. 42 Criminal Action 1987, on June 3, 1994, and the order denying post-trial motions on January 20, 1995. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Joseph E. Erb.
Commonwealth v. Chambers, 528 Pa. 558, 599 A.2d 630, 1991 Pa. LEXIS 238 (1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of robbery and murder in the first degree. Defendant's original death sentence was vacated and remanded due to the prosecutor's improper argument during the penalty phase. A new jury imposed the death penalty, and defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and first-degree murder. Defendant's original death sentence was vacated and remanded due to improper prosecutorial argument during the penalty phase. A second jury imposed the death penalty, and defendant sought review. The court held that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9711(h)(2) and (4), as amended in 1988, applied to all cases or appeals pending on the effective date and that when reviewing a death sentence, the court should affirm the sentence or vacate and remand for further proceedings. The court held that the prosecutorial misconduct had not descended to a level which violated all principles of justice and fairness of double jeopardy. The court held that the trial court had broad discretion in phrasing jury instructions so long as the law was clearly and accurately presented to the jury. The court held that a change of venue was not necessary because there had been a sufficient cooling-off period between the publicity and the trial. Because defendant's witness' statements were contradictory and harmful, it was proper to allow defendant's counsel to cross-examine defendant's own witness. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of robbery and murder in the first degree and sentenced him to death. The court held that the prosecutorial misconduct had not descended to a level which violated all principles of justice and fairness of double jeopardy. The court held that defendant's counsel could cross-examine defendant's witness because witness' statements were contradictory and harmful.

DOT v. Clayton


No. 78 Western District Appeal Docket, 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


546 Pa. 342; 684 A.2d 1060; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 2119


September 19, 1995, ARGUED  
November 1, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 969 C.D. 1987, Entered August 17, 1993, Affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Civil Division, at No. 6681 of 1986, Entered April 6, 1987. 157 Pa. Commw. 610, 630 A.2d 927 (1993). JUDGES BELOW: Hon. John F. Bell (C.C.P.). CRAIG, FRIEDMAN, SILVESTRI, S.J., JJ. (Cmwlth.).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which ruled that appellant's regulation that revoked the operating privilege upon the occurrence of a single epileptic episode was violative of due process.


OVERVIEW: Appellee driver suffered a grand mal epileptic seizure. His physician submitted a convulsive disorder form to appellant bureau in accordance with 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1518(b). Appellant recalled appellee's operating privileges on the basis that his seizure rendered him incompetent to drive for one year pursuant to 67 Pa. Code § 83.4. Both the trial court and the commonwealth court ruled that the regulation at issue was violative of due process. Relying on a line of cases decided by the U.S. Supreme Court, the court affirmed the commonwealth court. The court held that the regulation created an irrebuttable presumption that a person who had suffered one seizure was incompetent to drive for a period of one-year following the occurrence of the seizure. The court held that the essential elements of due process were notice and a meaningful opportunity to be heard. Though the regulation provided an opportunity for a de novo hearing on whether the operator suffered a seizure, it did not allow an opportunity to rebut the presumption of incompetence to drive. Thus, the de novo hearing was not a meaningful hearing and the regulation violated due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the commonwealth court that found appellant bureau's regulation that provided for the revocation of operating privileges upon one occurrence of an epileptic seizure violative of due process.

Appellant claimed that the separation of powers doctrine did not prohibit it from deciding a discrimination claim filed by a court officer employed by appellee court of common pleas. Commonwealth v. duPont


Nos. 81, 89, 91 & 96 E.D. Miscellaneous Docket 1996


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 564; 681 A.2d 1328; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1667


June 20, 1996, SUBMITTED  
August 19, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Commonwealth v. DuPont, 1999 PA Super 88, 730 A.2d 970, 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 813 (Pa. Super. Ct., 1999)
Related proceeding at In re Estate of duPont, 2009 PA Super 29, 966 A.2d 636, 2009 Pa. Super. LEXIS 34 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Interlocutory Appeal Under 42 Pa. C.S. § 726 (Extraordinary Jurisdiction) From the Order for a Competency Examination by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Jenkins, J.) at Criminal Division No. 26-96, entered March 18, 1996. JUDGES: Hon. Patricia H. Jenkins.



DISPOSITION: We remand the case to the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County for further consideration consistent with this Opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an interlocutory appeal of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) for a competency examination, on the ground that the amended 50 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7403(a) was unconstitutional. Petitioner argued that because the trial court expressed a doubt as to his competence by ordering a competency examination sua sponte, it was inappropriate to retain the presumption that he was competent to stand trial.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged and held without bail for murder. The trial court sua sponte ordered a competency examination, and designated two psychiatrists to conduct the examination. Thereafter, petitioner filed an emergency appeal requesting a stay of his competency examination. Petitioner argued that because the trial court expressed a doubt as to his competence by ordering a competency examination sua sponte, it was inappropriate to retain the presumption that he was competent to stand trial. The appeals court remanded the case for further proceedings. The court ruled that the amended statute, 50 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7403(a), only required a criminal defendant to demonstrate his incompetency by a preponderance of the evidence. The court noted that this new standard of proof was in accordance with federal and state supreme court cases that hold that requiring a criminal defendant to prove his incompetence to stand trial by a preponderance of the evidence did not violate due process. Thus, the court ruled that regardless of whether the trial court or petitioner raises the question of incompetency by requesting a competency examination, petitioner shouldered the burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The appeals court remanded the case for further proceedings. The court ruled that under state and federal constitutional law, a state may presume that the defendant is competent and require him to shoulder the burden of proving his incompetence by a preponderance of the evidence.

Commonwealth v. Jenner


No. 71 M.D. Appeal Docket 1994, No. 72 M.D. Appeal Docket 1994, No. 49 W.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 445; 681 A.2d 1266; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1500


January 26, 1995, ARGUED, SUBMITTED  
July 30, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 10, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, entered on December 3, 1993, at No. 108 Harrisburg, 1993, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Bradford Court, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 92 SA 075, entered on January 20, 1993.

Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, entered on December 23, 1993, at No. 00651 Harrisburg, 1992, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 91-11221 entered on September 10, 1992.

Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, entered on March 22, 1994, at No. 689 Pittsburgh, 1993, affirming the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division at No. 1109 2/3 of 1991, entered on April 7, 1993. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. William J. Franks (C.C.P.); Del Sole, Ford Elliott & Montgomery, JJ. (Super.).



DISPOSITION: THE JUDGMENTS OF SENTENCE ARE AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant defendants sought review of judgments from the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed convictions from the trial court for driving under the influence of alcohol or controlled substances related offenses, which subjected them to sentencing enhancement provisions due to prior convictions for driving on the public roads while their drivers' licenses were suspended.


OVERVIEW: Each appellant was cited for driving a motor vehicle on the public roads of Pennsylvania while their driver's licenses were suspended, and each license was suspended for a non-driving under the influence of alcohol or controlled substances (DUI) related offense, which subjected them to the sentencing enhancement provisions of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1543(b) when they were later arrested for DUI. Appellants surrendered their licenses pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1540(a). The court held that because the licenses were suspended in accordance with § 1543(b), the periods of suspension became effective immediately upon receipt of the certified conviction under § 1540(a). The application of § 1543(b) resulting in a suspension from the date of the DUI conviction until the date on which driving privileges were restored withstood equal-protection scrutiny. The court affirmed the convictions because once a driver was notified that his license was suspended under 75 Pa. 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731, he was subject to the enhanced sentencing provisions of § 1543(b) for the duration of any prior periods of suspension or revocation until the completion of the DUI-related suspension.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment against all three appellants because once a driver was notified that his license was suspended as a result of a conviction for driving under the influence, he was subject to the enhanced sentencing provisions.

 

82 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Barud


No. 64 W.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 297; 681 A.2d 162; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1501


September 18, 1995, ARGUED  
July 30, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Direct appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, dated September 19, 1994, at No. CC 9402512. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Walter R. Little.
Commonwealth v. Barud, 27 Pa. D. & C.4th 399, 1994 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 31 (1994)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the count of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of the driving under the influence statute § 3731(a)(5), holding that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was charged with one count of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(1) (incapable of safe driving); one count of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3731(a)(5); and a summary offense for failing to stop at a stop sign. Appellee filed omnibus pretrial motions and the trial court issued an opinion and order that granted appellee's pretrial motion that dismissed the second count, holding that § 3731(a)(5) was unconstitutional, and the Commonwealth appealed. The court held that without having required any proof that the person actually exceeded the legal limit of .10 percent at the time of driving, the statute swept unnecessarily broadly into activity which had not been declared unlawful and the statute completely failed to require any proof that the accused's blood alcohol level had exceeded the legal limit at the time of driving. The court affirmed the order of the trial court, holding that because § 3731(a)(5) had clearly, palpably, and plainly violated both the constitutions of the United States and of this Commonwealth, it was therefore unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court because the statute unnecessarily encompassed both lawful and unlawful conduct; failed to provide a reasonable standard by which a person could gauge their conduct; encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement; and failed to require proof that a person's blood alcohol content actually exceeded the legal limit at the time of driving, and therefore the statute was unconstitutional.

Carroll v. Ringgold Educ. Ass'n


No. 53 Western District Appeal Docket 1995


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 192; 680 A.2d 1137; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1495


March 4, 1996, ARGUED  
July 29, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered February 24, 1995, at No. 1486 C.D. 1994, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Civil Division, Entered June 7, 1994, at No. 94-2924. 655 A.2d 613 (1995). JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Debra O'Dell Seneca (C.C.P.). COLINS, P.J., DOYLE, MCGINLEY, SMITH, PELLEGRINI, FRIEDMAN, KELLEY, JJ. (Cmwlth.).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association filed a petition for allowance of appeal from the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the trial court decision and concluded that Act 88, 24 P.S. §§ 11-1101-A through 11-1172-A, removed public school employees from the scope of Public Employee Relations Act, 43 P.S. § 1101.1003.


OVERVIEW: The school district and the board of school directors were parties to a collective bargaining agreement with appellant association. After unsuccessful negotiations, appellant commenced a strike. Appellee secretary of education filed a complaint seeking an injunction to prevent the strike. The equity court granted injunctive relief and immediately issued a second order repeating the first order, but directed appellant and the board to engage in court-monitored bargaining. The intermediate appellate court reversed, concluding that Act 88, 43 P.S. § 11-1101-A et seq., removed public school employees from the scope of Public Employees Relation Act (PERA), 43 P.S. § 1101.1003, therefore the equity court could not order court-monitored bargaining. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that Act 88 was to be construed in pari materia with PERA as though the two sections were part of one statute. The court held that a court sitting in equity that had issued an injunction restraining a work stoppage and an order requiring rescheduling of school days also had authority to order a school board and a teachers association to enter into court-monitored negotiations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's decision, which reversed trial court decision and concluded that Act 88 removed public school employees from the scope of Public Employee Relations Act. The court opined that Act 88 was to be construed in pari materia with PERA as though the two sections are part of one statute. The court concluded that the equity court had authority to order court-monitored negotiations in this case.

Pa. State Ass'n of County Comm'Rs v. Commonwealth


No. 112 Western District Miscellaneous Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 324; 681 A.2d 699; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1487


September 30, 1994, SUBMITTED  
July 26, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Pa. State Ass'n of County Comm'rs v. Commonwealth, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1785 (Pa., Aug. 14, 2009)
Writ of mandamus denied, Motion denied by Pa. State Ass'n of County Comm'rs v. Commonwealth, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 2235 (Pa., Sept. 26, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Action in Mandamus Pursuant to 42 PA. C.A.S. § 721 (2). James C. Crumlish, Theodore Rogers, David W. Craig, JOHN A. MacPHAIL, Joseph T. Doyle, James Gardner Colins, and Madaline Palladino, JJ.).
County of Allegheny v. Commonwealth, 517 Pa. 65, 534 A.2d 760, 1987 Pa. LEXIS 843 (1987)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county commissioners sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent state to establish a state scheme of funding for the courts of Pennsylvania. 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 726, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15.


OVERVIEW: In 1987, the court held that the statutory scheme for county funding of the judicial system of Pennsylvania was unconstitutional pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, § 1. The court stayed its judgment to give respondent state an opportunity to enact funding legislation that would cure the constitutional defect. Nine years later, petitioner county commissioners sought a writ of mandamus to compel respondent to establish a state scheme of funding. The court granted the petition. The court held that the dispute was a matter of immediate public importance, and thus assumed jurisdiction pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 726. The court held that mandamus was appropriate because petitioners sought to compel the official performance of a ministerial act, or mandatory duty, and had no other remedy at law. The court held that the speech or debate clause, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15, did not insulate respondent from suit where respondent had been directed to remedy a constitutional defect in the scheme that funded the court system. The court held that the crucial function of the separation of powers principle was not separation per se, but the "checking" power each branch had over the others.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner county commissioners request for a writ of mandamus. The court held that Pennsylvania's speech and debate clause did not insulate the legislature from the court's authority to require the legislative branch to act in accord with the constitution.

Park Home v. City of Williamsport


No. 46 M.D. Appeal Docket 1995


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 94; 680 A.2d 835; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1454


January 24, 1996, ARGUED  
July 19, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered November 23, 1994, at No. 2742 C.D. 1993, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Civil Division, entered October 21, 1993, at No. 92-01, 607. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Kenneth D. Brown (C.C.P.). McGINLEY, KELLEY, JJ & RODGERS, SJ (CMWLTH).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-profit corporation sought review after the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed the order that denied appellant a permit to demolish a historic structure.


OVERVIEW: Appellant non-profit corporation applied for a permit to demolish a building in the historic district of appellee city. After the board denied appellant's request, appellant sought review in the trial court. The trial court affirmed the board's decision, as did the appellate court. The court affirmed. The court held that appellant had not met its burden of proof with respect to proof of an unconstitutional taking. The court noted that a member of appellant's board of directors testified that the board had not considered the possibility of selling the property as an alternative to demolition, and that there was no proof that the building was in such bad shape that it could jeopardize the safety of its occupants. The court held that the enabling statute, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8004(b), and the ordinance, Williamsport, Pa., Ordinance 4720, § 8 (1975), were not unconstitutionally vague. The court held that they both possessed the requisite degree of specificity such that they were not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant non-profit corporation a permit to demolish a historic structure. The court held that the statute and ordinance at issue were not unconstitutionally vague and that appellant had not met its burden of proof with respect to a taking.

Commonwealth v. Fisher


No. 48 E.D. Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


545 Pa. 233; 681 A.2d 130; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1308


January 22, 1996, ARGUED; January 22, 1996, REARGUED  
June 25, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied September 6, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1733.
Reargument denied by Commonwealth v. Fisher, 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1733 (Pa., Sept. 6, 1996)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Fisher, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 612 (Pa., Mar. 30, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, at 1134-88, Dated May 26, 1992. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Samuel W. Salus, II.
Commonwealth v. Fisher, 527 Pa. 345, 591 A.2d 710, 1991 Pa. LEXIS 117 (1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which entered his jury conviction for capital murder and imposed a sentence of death, by virtue of the automatic direct appeal from a judgment of sentence of death. 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 722(4), 9711(h)(1); Pa. R. App. P. 702(b), 1941. Appellant alleged the trial court erred in admitting victim impact evidence during the sentencing hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder for the shooting death of his girlfriend. The jury found that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances and sentenced appellant to death, after which he challenged the judgment. On appeal, the court found that the evidence was sufficient to convict appellant. The victim's death was witnessed by appellant's friend, who testified at appellant's trial approximately eight years after the murder. The delay in trial was the result of appellant's fleeing to another state. Further, other witnesses supplied relevant circumstantial evidence. Therefore, the court affirmed appellant's conviction. However, the court vacated appellant's sentence and remanded for a new sentencing hearing, finding that the trial court erred in admitting victim impact evidence, in the form of the testimony of the victim's mother. Further, the trial court gave the jurors confusing instructions in telling them that the evidence could be considered "mentally" without considering it an aggravating circumstance. This error interjected an arbitrary factor into the sentencing decision of the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of murder in the first degree, but vacated the judgment of sentence of death and remanded for a new sentencing hearing. The court found all of appellant's arguments regarding the guilt phase of his trial meritless, but agreed that the capital sentencing scheme in effect at the time of his trial precluded the admission of victim impact testimony.

Hoffman v. Township of Whitehall


No. 124 M.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


544 Pa. 499; 677 A.2d 1200; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1283


December 7, 1995, ARGUED  
June 19, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, dated October 25, 1994, at No. 93-C-2978. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Carol McGinley (C.C.P.).

Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County dated October 27, 1994 at No. 93-C-2978. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Carol McGinley (C.C.P.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant veteran challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which found the Veterans' Preference Act, 51 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7101to be unconstitutional in favor of appellees, employer and its officers.


OVERVIEW: Appellant veteran was employed by appellee employer as a police detective. He sought promotion to the position of lieutenant. Appellant took the necessary written examination and received a passing score. Appellant ranked fourth among the applicants and was eliminated from the final round of three who were considered for the position. Appellant contended that his test score should have been raised 10 points due to the Veteran's Preference Act (Act), 51 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7101 et seq., placing him in the top three. Appellees contended that the Act was unconstitutional and the trial court agreed. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that a similar provision had been declared unconstitutional in a prior decision and that the reenactment of the 10 point preference was similarly unconstitutional as it violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 7 and federal equal protection and due process provisions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court against appellant veteran because that a similar provision had been declared unconstitutional in a prior decision and the reenactment of the 10 point preference was similarly unconstitutional as it violated the Pennsylvania Constitution and federal equal protection and due process provisions.

Commonwealth v. Abdul-Salaam


No. 102 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


544 Pa. 514; 678 A.2d 342; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1276


December 7, 1995, ARGUED  
June 18, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Stay granted by Commonwealth v. Abdul-Salaam, 546 Pa. 240, 684 A.2d 539, 1996 Pa. LEXIS 2078 (1996)
Writ of certiorari denied Abdul-Salaam v. Pennsylvania, 520 U.S. 1157, 117 S. Ct. 1337, 137 L. Ed. 2d 496, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 2133 (1997)
Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. Abdul-Salaam, 786 A.2d 974, 2001 Pa. LEXIS 2764 (Pa., 2001)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Stay denied by Commonwealth v. Abdul-Salaam, 2002 Pa. LEXIS 2509 (Pa., Dec. 4, 2002)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Abdul-Salaam v. Beard, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45506 (M.D. Pa., Aug. 11, 2005)
Related proceeding at Abdul-Salaam v. Pa. Dep't of Corr., 2010 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 880 (2010)
Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. Seifullah Abdul-Salaam, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 766 (Pa., Apr. 5, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Pennsylvania, dated March 24, 1995, at 94-1499 Criminal Term. JUDGE BELOW: Hon. Kevin Hess.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction and sentence of death imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania) for murder of the first degree, robbery, and conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder of the first degree, robbery, and conspiracy, and sentenced to death. He challenged both on appeal. The court found that defendant's defective affidavit issue was moot as there was a prima facie case finding at the preliminary hearing. The court held that there was sufficient evidence to establish an independent basis for in-court eyewitness identifications and thus they were properly admitted. A statement made by defendant was found to be an unsolicited and gratuitous utterance, and thus was not subject to suppression. Evidence found in a briefcase was also properly admitted, as it was properly searched under a search warrant. Because the trial court found that eyewitness identification expert testimony was not necessary for defense purposes, it properly denied its payment. Even though the state did not give timely notice of aggravating circumstances at defendant's arraignment as required by Pa. R. Crim. P. 352, the related evidence did not need to be excluded as defendant was on constructive notice of such circumstances. The court found that the jury charge and death sentence were proper. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence for murder of the first degree, robbery, and conspiracy. The court found that eyewitness testimony was properly admitted as it had an independent basis, and an unsolicited and gratuitous utterance by defendant was not subject to suppression. As defendant had constructive knowledge of aggravating circumstances, the fact that the state served the same to him late was not prejudicial.

Commonwealth v. Beasley


No. 29 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


544 Pa. 554; 678 A.2d 773; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1279


September 1, 1995, SUBMITTED  
June 18, 1996, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Denied August 6, 1996, Reported at: 1996 Pa. LEXIS 1570. Certiorari Denied March 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1731.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, dated November 18, 1991, denying appellant's second petition for relief pursuant to the Post Conviction Relief Act collaterally attacking a judgment of sentence of death at Information Nos. 2175-78, July Term, 1980. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Alfred F. Sabo.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9546 (1988), in conjunction with defendant's conviction of murder of the first degree and possession of an instrument of crime and the penalty of death.


OVERVIEW: Upon his conviction and death sentence for first degree murder and possession of an instrument of crime, defendant filed many appeals. He filed a relief petition under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9541-9546 (1988), which the lower court denied. Defendant challenged the ruling and argued that he was entitled to relief under the PCRA because his allegations had not been previously litigated, and while they were waived, it was due to ineffective assistance of counsel, and thus the anti-waiver provision, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9543(a)(3)(iii) (1988), applied. The court held that defendant failed to prove any ineffectiveness. The attorney did not commit error in following defendant's directive to not present mitigation evidence, nor in failing to raise a meritless objection. Counsel was not ineffective for failing to object to state's closing argument, as it was not improper and was based on legitimate inferences. As defendant failed to establish pervasive pretrial publicity, counsel properly did not seek a change of venue. As defendant did not prove ineffectiveness, he was not eligible for PCRA relief, and the court affirmed the lower court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order which denied defendant's petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA). The court found that under the PCRA, while his allegations had not been litigated, they were waived, and because defendant did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel, the anti-waiver provisions of the PCRA did not apply and thus defendant was ineligible for relief.

Public Advocate v. Phila. Gas Comm'n


No. 98 E.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


544 Pa. 129; 674 A.2d 1056; 1996 Pa. LEXIS 814


October 23, 1995, ARGUED  
April 22, 1996, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Commonwealth Court entered January 14, 1994 at No. 2545 C.D. 92 reversing in part and affirming in part the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Civil Division, entered October 16, 1992, at No. 2961, January Term, 1992. 161 Pa. Commw. 428, 637 A.2d 676 (1994). JUDGES BELOW: Hon. G. Craig Lord, (C.C.P.). Craig, Doyle, Colins, Palladino, (Cmwlth.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the gas commission, the gas producing utility, and Philadelphia Facilities Management Corporation, challenged a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), in a utility rate action, where the court had ruled that an annual payment from appellant gas utility to the city constituted a tax which was imposed without the city properly exercising its taxing powers, and thus, was arbitrary and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant gas commission permitted appellant utility's rate increase for fiscal year 1991-92. Appellee public advocate represented residential customers in gas rate proceedings, and challenged the decision before the trial court. That court affirmed the gas commission's decision that the utility's $ 18,000,000 payment to the City of Philadelphia did not violate the Pennsylvania Constitution or Pennsylvania law. The trial court held that the payment represented a reasonable return on the city's equity in the utility. Appellee contended the payment was an unconstitutional tax. The commonwealth court reversed, and held that the payment was not proportionate to the ratepayers' use of the gas works, the annual payment constituted a tax which was imposed without the city properly exercising its taxing powers, and thus, was arbitrary and unconstitutional. The court reversed. The overall rate approved by the gas commission for the gas works' 1991-92 fiscal year was constitutional since it was just and reasonable and since the fixed annual payment was rationally related to the city's asserted equity in the gas works.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the commonwealth court, which had reversed the trial court's ruling that the annual payment from appellant gas utility to the city was constitutional and a reasonable return on equity, because the city owned the utility, and the payment constituted a rational return on equity.

Balent v. City of Wilkes-Barre


No. 134 Middle District Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


542 Pa. 555; 669 A.2d 309; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1425


August 30, 1995, SUBMITTED  
December 27, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court entered September 22, 1994 at No. 2322 C.D. 1993, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County dated June 8, 1993 at No. 5123-C of 1983. JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Hugh F. Mundy (C.C.P.). Smith, Pellegrini and Kelton, JJ (Cmwlth).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania that affirmed the judgment of the trial court which denied appellant's motion for post-trial relief and affirmed a verdict in favor of appellees, building owners, in an action by appellees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, regarding the demolition of their building.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, building owners', building sustained considerable structural damage. Appellant city notified appellees to bring the building up to code or it would be razed. The owners did not respond. The city sent a second and third notice. Pursuant to its police powers, the city razed appellees' building. Appellees' first eminent domain action was dismissed by the court. Appellees then filed an action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claiming that the city's demolition of their building deprived them of a right, privilege and immunity secured by U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV. The city filed a motion for summary judgment asserting that appellees' claim was barred by either res judicata or collateral estoppel, based on the unsuccessful eminent domain action. The trial court denied the motion and damages were awarded in appellees' favor. On appeal, this court concluded that the § 1983 action was barred under the theory of res judicata because both actions by appellees involved compensation for the same property and the same exercise of power by the city. The trial court erred by affirming the denial of the city's summary judgment motion, so its order was reversed and the case dismissed.


OUTCOME: The order of the court that affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant city's motion for post-trial relief and affirmed a verdict in favor of appellees, building owners, was reversed and the case was dismissed because appellees' action was barred by res judicata.

Commonwealth v. Burnsworth


No. 54 Western District Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


543 Pa. 18; 669 A.2d 883; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1430


September 18, 1995, ARGUED  
December 27, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence entered July 8, 1994, Court of Common Pleas, Erie County, Criminal Division at Nos. 2738 and 2739 of 1993. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Shad Connelly (C.C.P.).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, sought review of the judgment of sentence entered by the Erie County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which sentenced defendant to 6 to 12 months imprisonment, followed by 48 months of probation, refusing to impose a minimum mandatory sentence as required by 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7508(a)(1)(i), (ii), and (iii); the trial court instead declared the statute unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of growing marijuana plants, but the trial court refused to impose a minimum mandatory sentence pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7508(a)(1)(i), (ii), and (iii), finding the statute unconstitutional. The court found that statutory interpretation was guided by the Statutory Construction Act, 1 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1901-1991, and that a strong presumption of constitutionality existed. The court found that when a statute was clear and unambiguous, it was given its plain meaning. The court held that the statutory language which discussed marijuana plants included the growing of any number of plants. The court found that there was a rational basis for the statute. The court reversed and remanded the case, holding that the statute was constitutional and valid, and there was a rational basis for imposing a minimum mandatory sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision not to impose a minimum mandatory sentence on defendant, holding that there was a rational basis for the statute, the mandatory sentence was constitutional, and it was a valid means of deterring the growing of marijuana plants.

 

93 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Wilkinsburg Educ. Ass'n v. Sch. Dist. of Wilkinsburg & Bd. of Sch. Dirs. of the Sch. Dist. of Wilkinsburg


No. 55 Western District Appeal Docket 1995


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


542 Pa. 335; 667 A.2d 5; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1053


September 18, 1995, ARGUED  
October 27, 1995, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied December 12, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 1394.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Order of the Commonwealth Court entered July 17, 1995, at No. 1032 C.D. 1995, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division, entered March 30, 1995, at No. GD 95-5174. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Judith L.A. Friedman, (C.C.P.), MCGINLEY, KELLEY, SILVESTRI, S.J., JJ. (Cmwlth.)



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school board challenged a decision from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee union's petition for injunctive relief thereby enjoining appellant from entering into a contract with a private corporation to operate and manage a school.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school board wanted to enter into a contract with a private corporation to operate and manage a school. Appellee union contended that the Public School Code of 1949, 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1 et seq., did not authorize such contract. Appellee initiated an action for injunctive relief against appellant that sought to enjoin appellant from entering into such contract. Without holding an evidentiary hearing on such issue, the trial court issued a preliminary injunction. Appellant contended that a hearing was necessary. On appeal, the court dissolved the temporary injunction and remanded for an evidentiary hearing. Without a hearing, appellant was unable to introduce evidence and explain the reasons for the proposed contract. Appellant was also unable to argue that the Code did allow for subcontracting of teachers. Appellant contended that if the Code did not allow for such subcontracting, then it conflicted with Pa. Const. art. III, § 14, which required appellant to provide a through and efficient educational system.


OUTCOME: The court dissolved the injunction, which enjoined appellant school board from entering into the contract and remanded for an evidentiary hearing. The trial court abused its discretion issuing the preliminary injunction without first holding an evidentiary hearing. Appellant was denied the opportunity to present its reasons for proposing the contract.

In re Reese


No. 18 E.D. Appeal Docket 1995


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


542 Pa. 114; 665 A.2d 1162; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 972


April 27, 1995, ARGUED  
October 17, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Opinion and Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Dated March 29, 1995, at No. 1405-C of 1995. Hon. Joseph M. Augello (C.C.P.)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens sought review of the judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee mayor's objections to appellant's petition to recall appellee, set aside appellant's petition to recall, and directed the county board of elections not to place the recall question on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellant citizens sought to remove appellee mayor. They followed the procedures of Kingston, Pa. Home Rule Charter §§ 704-05, which provided for removal by petition and referendum. Appellee brought suit to have the petition set aside on the grounds that it was unconstitutional. The trial court set aside the petition and ordered the Board of Elections not to place the recall issue on the ballot. The court affirmed the trial court's order. The court held that §§ 704-05 violated Pa. Const. art. VI, § 7. Art. VI, § 7 indisputably applied to all elected officers and set forth in unambiguous language the exclusive method, absent impeachment, conviction of crime or misbehavior in office, of removing elected officers. In providing for recall, the city exceeded the powers conferred by Pa. Const. art. XI, § 2 and the Home Rule Charter and Optional Plans Law, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1-101 et seq.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the trial court's judgment sustaining appellee mayor's objections, setting aside the recall petition, and directing the board of elections not to place the issue on the ballot. The court held that the city charter provisions calling for recall were unconstitutional. The Pennsylvania Constitution provided that the only method for removing an elected official was by the Governor on the address of two-thirds of the Senate.

 

95 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Curtis v. Kline (appeal of Dep't of Pub. Welfare)


No. 6 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


542 Pa. 249; 666 A.2d 265; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 925


September 21, 1995, ARGUED  
October 10, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Domestic Relations Section, Entered January 12, 1994 at No. 1012 N. 1984, Granting Defendant's Petition to Modify and Terminate Support. JUDGES BELOW: Hon. James P. MacElree, II.
Curtis v. Kline, 25 Pa. D. & C.4th 276, 1994 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 81 (1994)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public welfare department challenged a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee father's petition to terminate his child support obligation for his college age children. The court also held that 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4327(a) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee father was ordered to pay child support for his three children. Two of the children began attending college, so appellee filed a petition to terminate his support obligation as to those two children. The trial court granted appellee's petition because it found that 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4327(a), which required a divorced parent to provide college educational support, was unconstitutional. Appellant public welfare department intervened contending that such statute did not violate equal protection principles. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. Section 4327(a) violated equal protection principles because it treated the children of divorced parents and married parents unequally. Under the statute, divorced parents were obligated to pay the educational costs of their children after the children reached age 18. Married parents had no such obligation. There was no legitimate state reason for treating children of divorced parents differently than children of married parents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision terminating appellee father's child support obligation and the finding that the educational support statute was unconstitutional. The statute violated equal protection principles by treating the children of divorced parents and the children of married parents differently when there was no legitimate reason for such distinction.

Commonwealth v. Bricker

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee citizen's motion to quash a flag desecration charge. The trial court declared the Pennsylvania flag desecration statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(A), unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Based on observations made by a state trooper while in appellee citizen's apartment regarding her use of the United States flag, appellee was arrested and charged with desecrating the flag under the Pennsylvania flag desecration statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2102(A). Appellant state challenged the trial court order, which granted appellee's motion to quash the flag desecration charge, upon finding § 2102(A) unconstitutional. On direct appeal, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 722(7), the court affirmed the trial court judgment as it found § 2102(A) unconstitutionally applied to appellee citizen, and reversed as it found § 2102(A) unconstitutional as written. The court held that because appellee's decorative use of the flag was non-expressive, the court was not concerned with the interest appellant state had in regulating free expression, as the U.S. Const. amend. I freedom of speech right was not implicated. The court held that appellant must satisfy constitutional scrutiny before it can tell the citizens of this commonwealth what pictures they may hang on their walls or what symbols they may display in the sanctity of their homes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment as it found the flag desecration statute unconstitutionally applied to appellee citizen, and reversed as it found the legislation unconstitutional as written. The court held that because the use of the flag was non-expressive, the court was not concerned with the interest appellant state had in regulating free expression, as the constitutionally protected freedom of speech right was not implicated.

Commonwealth v. Swinehart


No. 90 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


541 Pa. 500; 664 A.2d 957; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 623


January 25, 1995, ARGUED  
August 29, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court dated May 24, 1994, at No. 135 Philadelphia 1994, which Affirmed the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, at No. 4371-94, dated January 19, 1994. 434 Pa. Super. 123, 642 A.2d 504 (1994). JUDGES BELOW: Hon. Lawrence A. Brown (C.C.P.); Beck, Popovich & Hoffman, JJ. (Super.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Pennsylvania Superior Court, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court which found him in civil contempt and sentenced him for criminal contempt after he refused to testify upon being granted immunity pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5947 for testimony in the murder trial of an alleged co-conspirator.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed the decision of the superior court, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court which found him in civil contempt and sentenced him for criminal contempt after he was granted immunity for testimony in the murder trial of an alleged co-conspirator but refused to testify. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction after reviewing the text and history of Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5947, caselaw from other states, and policy considerations. The court held that the privilege against self-incrimination under art. I, § 9 was more expansive than that under U.S. Const. amend. V; it was not, however, so expansive that it would require transactional immunity rather than the use and derivative use immunity provided in § 5947. The court held that in the prosecution of a defendant subsequent to his immunized testimony, the evidence offered by the commonwealth would have to be reviewed with the most careful scrutiny, that is, the commonwealth would have to prove, of record, by the heightened standard of clear and convincing evidence, that the evidence upon which the subsequent prosecution was brought arose wholly from independent sources.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's criminal contempt conviction, holding that the Pennsylvania Constitution's privilege against self-incrimination, although greater than the Fifth Amendment's, was satisfied by statutory use and derivative use immunity, and the commonwealth would have to prove, by clear and convincing evidence, that its evidence in any prosecution subsequent to the immunized testimony arose wholly from independent sources.

Commonwealth v. Miller


No. 64 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


541 Pa. 531; 664 A.2d 1310; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 613


January 26, 1995, ARGUED  
August 25, 1995, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied February 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1093.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division, at Nos. 2775, 2775(a), 2787, 2787(a) C.D. 1992, dated April 7, 1994. JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Jeannine Turgeon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, by direct appeal, challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to death, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(h)(1), following his convictions for two first degree murders, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502(a), and for kidnapping, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2901(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his death sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that under its duty to review the sufficiency of the evidence in capital cases, the evidence, when viewed in the light most favorable to the commonwealth, amply supported the convictions. Evidence of the rape and attempted murder of another victim was admissible to demonstrate a common scheme to establish identity and malice. The evidence of one victim's pregnancy was relevant to a physical characteristic in the common scheme and was necessary to identify the victim. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying a bench trial or appellant's resulting motion for recusal. Appellant's statements were voluntarily and knowingly made. Trial counsel was not ineffective for failing to present a diminished capacity defense based on appellant's irrelevant impulsiveness, failing to present an unsupportable voluntary intoxication defense, or failing to object to the prosecutor's permissible sentencing arguments for deterrence and retribution. Sufficient evidence supported the aggravating circumstances found by the jury. The sentences of death were not illegal, excessive, or disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the death sentence, where there was sufficient evidence to support the convictions, evidence was properly admitted to show a common scheme, appellant's requests for a bench trial and the judge's recusal were properly denied, there was no ineffective assistance in counsel's failure to present a voluntary intoxication or diminished capacity defense or to object to the prosecutor's arguments for deterrence and retribution.

Commonwealth v. Graham


No. 38 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


541 Pa. 173; 661 A.2d 1367; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 497


January 26, 1995, ARGUED  
July 18, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section entered May 3, 1988, at Nos. 3951, 3954, 3959, 3960, 3964, and 3967 August Term, 1987.

JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Robert A. Latrone.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought an automatic direct review of judgments of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section (Pennsylvania), sentencing him to six death sentences after conviction on seven counts of first degree murder and abuse of corpse.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of seven counts of first degree murder and abuse of corpse and was sentenced to consecutive sentences of one to two years imprisonment for each of the seven abuse of corpse convictions and consecutive to those sentences, a life sentence followed by six death sentences consecutive to the other sentences. The appeal was an automatic direct review, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 722(4) and 9711(h), although appellant expressed his desire that no appeal be litigated. The court held that under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9721(a), a trial court generally had discretion to determine whether to make sentences concurrent or consecutive to other sentences being imposed or previously imposed, but when it was formally imposing a sentence of death determined in accordance with proceedings pursuant to § 9711, the trial court had no discretion to order that such sentence be consecutive to any other sentences being imposed or previously imposed. The court vacated the judgment in so far as they directed that the sentences were consecutive to the terms of years and the life term imposed on the murder counts and it affirmed the judgment in all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's judgment that appellant's consecutive imprisonment sentences be followed by a life sentence which was to be followed by six death sentences consecutive to the other sentences. The court held that the trial court had no discretion to order that the death sentence be consecutive to the other sentences imposed.

DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judges of the court of common pleas challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a lower court holding that appellee county was not responsible for the payment of attorney's fees appellant incurred while in litigation.

(Hon. F. Joseph Leahey).

Brickhouse v. Spring-Ford Area Sch. Dist.


No. 31 E.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


540 Pa. 176; 656 A.2d 483; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 223


October 20, 1994, ARGUED  
April 4, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court, dated May 3, 1993 at No. 0293 C.D. 1992 (Reargument denied June 25, 1993), Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, dated April 16, 1992 at No. 90-20290. JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. William T. Nicholas (C.C.P.); McGinley, Kelly & Kelton, JJ. (Super.).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the decision of the trial court and which found that appellee veteran had to be hired for a teaching position pursuant to the Veterans' Preference Act, 51 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7104.


OVERVIEW: Appellee veteran was denied a teaching position with appellant school district by the trial court. The appellate court reversed. Appellant sought review. The court determined that simply being a veteran was not enough under the Veterans' Preference Act (Act), 51 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7104, and that the veteran had to be, to some degree, qualified for the desired position. The court found that a veteran seeking to take advantage of the preference mandated by the Act had to be able to demonstrate his ability to perform the job at the level of skill and with the expertise demanded by appellant because such a demonstration established that he would be able to perform the job duties properly. The court also determined that appellant was entitled to set its hiring requirements at levels that reasonably related to the duties to be performed in order to ensure that the person hired was competent. The decision of the appellate court was reversed, and the decision of the trial court was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed. Appellee veteran, who sought preference under the Veterans' Preference Act, had to be qualified to perform the job at the level of expertise demanded by appellant school district, and appellant was free to set hiring requirements that reasonably related to the job's duties to ensure that the person hired was competent.

Post-conviction relief dismissed at, Decision reached on appeal by Commonwealth v. Walker, 613 Pa. 601, 36 A.3d 1, 2011 Pa. LEXIS 2893 (2011)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Walker v. Wetzel, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 107295 (E.D. Pa., Feb. 20, 2015)

JUDGE BELOW: Hon. Eugene H. Clarke, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that sentenced defendant to death upon convicting him of first-degree murder, aggravated assault, recklessly endangering another person, criminal trespass, and carrying firearms on a public street.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder, aggravated assault, recklessly endangering another person, criminal trespass, possession of an instrument of crime, and carrying firearms on a public street. Defendant was sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant argued the verdict was erroneous because he acted under a sudden and intense passion that made it impossible for him to act with the necessary intent required to establish first-degree murder. The reviewing court found that after defendant threatened to kill his ex-girlfriend, defendant drove to her residence and waited in his car for an extended period of time. Defendant then broke into the house and shot two individuals. The evidence was sufficient to establish that defendant acted with malice aforethought and with a specific intent to kill. Even if defendant acted under the heat of passion, a sufficient amount of time for cooling had elapsed between defendant made the threatening call and the actual murder. The court also found that defendant was not acting in self-defense because defendant went uninvited and armed to the house in the middle of the night and broke through the front door. The decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed because the evidence was sufficient to establish that defendant acted with malice aforethought and with specific intent to kill. The court also found defendant was not acting in self-defense because defendant went uninvited to the house armed with a gun.

Pa. State Police v. Pa. State Troopers' Ass'n


No. 55 Middle District Appeal Docket 1994, No. 56 Middle District Appeal Docket 1994, No. 57 Middle District Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


540 Pa. 66; 656 A.2d 83; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 199; 149 L.R.R.M. 2877


December 8, 1994, CONSOLIDATED APPEALS ARGUED  
March 21, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered at 980 C.D. 1991, on November 8, 1993, reversing the arbitrator's award entered April 1, 1991. 159 Pa. Commw. 489, 633 A.2d 1278 (1993). JUDGE(S) BELOW: Arbitrator Lang; Pellegrini, Smith & Doyle, JJ. (Comwlth.).

Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered at 2494 C.D. 1992, on November 10, 1993, affirming the arbitrator's award entered October 23, 1992. 159 Pa. Commw. 592, 633 A.2d 1330 (1993). JUDGE(S) BELOW: Arbitrator Schwartz; Smith, Doyle, JJ. & Lord, S. J. (Cmwlth.).

Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court Entered at 620 C.D. 1991, on November 15, 1993, reversing the arbitrator's award entered February 20, 1991. 159 Pa. Commw. 628, 634 A.2d 270 (1993). JUDGE(S) BELOW: Arbitrator Stolenberg; Smith, Pellegrini, JJ & Kelton, S. J. (Cmwlth.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three appeals were consolidated from decisions by Pennsylvania trial courts that reviewed arbitration awards in grievance actions by police and fire personnel.


OVERVIEW: Three appeals in grievance actions by police and fire personnel were consolidated because they raised the same issue of whether the correct scope of review was applied in reviewing grievance arbitration awards. The court found that the lower courts had not applied the same scope of review as to the arbitration awards. One lower court had used the narrow certiorari scope of review. On the other hand, the essence test, used by two of the lower courts, permitted a court to vacate an arbitrator's award if the court found that the award did not draw its essence from the collective bargaining agreement. The court held that the essence test under the Uniform Arbitration Act, Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7301-7320, should not have been applied merely because the cases included police and fire personnel. The court held that 42 Pa. Stat. 217.1-217.10 governed the cases and, thus, the essence test should not have been employed in the review. Accordingly, the court reversed the orders of the lower courts that relied on the essence test and reinstated the arbitrators' awards. The court affirmed the lower court's order in the other case, holding that the narrow certiorari scope of review was proper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower courts' orders in two cases and affirmed the order in the other case. The court held that the narrow certiorari scope of review was proper and that the essence test should not have been employed in the review.

Magazine Publrs. of Am. v. Dep't of Revenue


No. 119 Middle District Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 563; 654 A.2d 519; 1995 Pa. LEXIS 50; 23 Media L. Rep. 1337


December 7, 1993, ARGUED  
January 18, 1995, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court denying Appellants' application for summary relief, dated November 25, 1992, at No. 74 M.D. 92. 151 Pa. Commw. 592, 618 A.2d 1056 (1992). JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Bernard L. McGinley.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant magazine publishers challenged a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied their request for declaratory and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellant magazine publishers brought an action against appellees, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and Pennsylvania Department of Internal Revenue, that sought declaratory and injunctive relief. Appellants challenged the constitutionality of 72 P.S. § 7204(30), an amendment to the tax code that deleted the sales and use tax exemption for magazines. The trial court denied appellants relief. Appellants contended that the amendment violated their rights to freedom of speech under U.S. Const. amend. I and Pa. Const. art. I, § 7, freedom of the press, equal protection of the laws under U.S. Const. amend XIV, § 1 and Pa. Const. art. I, § 26, and uniformity of taxation under Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The court affirmed the trial court's order and held that the trial court applied the § 7204(30) in a content-neutral manner. The court also held that there was no abridgment of appellants' rights under art. I, § 7 because publications were exempt from generally applicable sales tax on the basis of format and frequency and not content. The court concluded that the sales tax, as applied to magazines, did not violate appellants' federal or state constitutional rights to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that denied appellant magazine publishers' petition for injunctive and declaratory relief and ruled that the trial court applied the applicable statute in a content-neutral manner. The court held that there was no abridgment of appellants' rights to freedom of speech because publications were exempt from the generally applicable sales tax on the basis of format and frequency and not content.

Commonwealth v. Brion


No. 62 Middle District Appeal Docket 1993


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 256; 652 A.2d 287; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 856


January 25, 1994, ARGUED  
December 30, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on January 12, 1989, at No. 204 Harrisburg 1988, Reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Criminal Division, Entered on February 19, 1988, at No. 87-10,271. 381 Pa. Super. 83, 552 A.2d 1105 (1989). JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Thomas C. Raup (C.C.P.) Tamilia, Popovich & Hoffman, JJ. (Super.).



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, Judgment reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which vacated the trial court's decision granting defendant's motion for a new trial based on its finding that conversations electronically recorded in defendant's home were unlawfully admitted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession and delivery of marijuana. He sought to suppress the tape recording of a transaction between himself and the confidential informant, which occurred in defendant's home. The district attorney authorized the interception, but no judicial approval was obtained. The appellate court vacated the trial court's order granting defendant a new trial. The court reversed the appellate court's order and remanded for a new trial, holding that one can reasonably expect that his right to privacy will not be violated in his home through the use of any electronic surveillance. There was no evidence to suggest that defendant did not have an expectation of privacy within his home. Because there was no determination of probable cause by a neutral judicial authority, the consensual body wire violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 8, and the tape recording of the transaction in defendant's home should have been suppressed. The court declined to consider whether the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5704, was unconstitutional and held that there was no good faith exception to the exclusionary rule.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order, which vacated the trial court's order granting defendant's motion for a new trial, because the electronic interception of the drug transaction in his home was unlawful and should have been suppressed. There was no determination of probable cause by a neutral judicial authority to permit the recording, thus, the electronic surveillance in defendant's home violated his constitutional rights.

Commonwealth v. Jones


No. 84 E.D. Appeal Docket 1985


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 222; 651 A.2d 1101; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 818


October 22, 1990, SUBMITTED; January 25, 1994, REARGUED  
December 28, 1994, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Commonwealth v. Jones, 541 Pa. 351, 663 A.2d 142, 1995 Pa. LEXIS 428 (1995)
Writ of certiorari denied Jones v. Pennsylvania, 516 U.S. 835, 133 L. Ed. 2d 65, 116 S. Ct. 113, 1995 U.S. LEXIS 5697 (1995)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Jones, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1326 (Pa., June 21, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section entered June 6, 1985, at Nos. 2486, 2487 & 2491 October Term 1980. JUDGE(S) BELOW: Hon. Robert A. Latrone, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502, two counts of arson endangering persons under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3301(a), and one count of arson endangering property under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3301(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of murder and arson after he was seen running from a house in which two women were found bound, gagged, and burnt to death. Defendant sustained serious burns. Defendant appealed, claiming that the trial court erroneously failed to suppress statements he gave to the police because they were involuntary due to his condition when they were made. The court affirmed, finding that the statement was voluntary because the factual findings were supported by the doctor's testimony that defendant was alert, cooperative, and unimpaired by pain drugs when interviewed by the police. Defendant's claim of error based on the trial court's failure to suppress crime scene evidence seized without a warrant lacked merit because the trial court properly applied the plain view exception to the warrant requirement. There was no error in refusing mistrial, where the trial court properly exercised its discretion to allow the commonwealth to ask a hypothetical question, defendant suffered no prejudice from the prosecutor remarks, and the jury charge on voluntary manslaughter was proper where the trial court clearly told the jury that its opinion was not binding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence for aggravated murders and arson because the sentence was not excessive and there was ample evidence of his guilt and aggravating circumstances. The trial court properly refused to suppress defendant's statements where the doctor testified he was alert and unimpaired when he spoke to police, and properly denied a mistrial where defendant was not prejudiced by the challenged actions.

Mahony v. Twp. of Hampton


No. 30 W.D. Appeal Docket 1994


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 193; 651 A.2d 525; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 749


September 19, 1994, ARGUED  
December 16, 1994, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application for Reargument Denied February 10, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Pa. LEXIS 78.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 1732 C.D. 1992, dated September 30, 1993, affirming the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvnaia, Civil Division, at No. SA 1611 of 1987, dated May 27, 1988, and July 9, 1992. 158 Pa. Cmwlth. 681, 632 A.2d 1102 (1993).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant gas well owner sought review of the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's decision upholding appellee township's zoning ordinance prohibiting a private enterprise from operating gas wells in residential districts but permitting public operation of such wells.


OVERVIEW: Appellee township's zoning officer denied appellant gas well owner's application for a variance to operate a gas well under appellee's zoning ordinance which permitted, in residential "A" districts, the use of municipal and public utility facilities to produce gas, but prohibited operation of gas wells by private entities. Appellant appealed to the township's zoning hearing board, the court of common pleas, and the appellate court, without obtaining relief. On review, appellant challenged the distinction between public and private operations, alleging that such a distinction was arbitrary and unreasonable and, hence, an invalid exercise of the township's police powers. The court reversed, holding the justifications advanced by appellee to support its prohibition of private operation were not supported by the record, the distinction between private and public operation was arbitrary, the means were not reasonably related to the ends sought to be attained, and the restrictions were patently beyond the necessities of the case because there was not a scintilla of evidence that a municipality or public utility could produce gas any more safely than a private operator.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment upholding appellee township's zoning ordinance, holding that the ordinance was an invalid exercise of the police power because it was unreasonable, oppressive, and its prohibition of private gas production did not bear a real and substantial relation to the health, safety, and welfare of the community. The case was remanded for entry of judgment in favor of appellant gas well owner.

Commonwealth v. Riedel


No. 66 Middle District Appeal Docket 1993


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 172; 651 A.2d 135; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 690


April 7, 1994, SUBMITTED  
December 1, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Superior Court entered October 22, 1992 at 70 Harrisburg 1992, affirming the Judgment of Sentence entered January 27, 1992 in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, York County, No. 2183 C.A. 1991. Judge(s) Below: Hon. John C. Uhler (C.C.P.); Tamilia, Johnson & Hester, JJ. (Super.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court that affirmed his conviction from the Court of Common Pleas, York County (Pennsylvania), for driving under the influence under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3731(a)(1) and (a)(4).


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged the lower court's judgment that affirmed his conviction for driving under the influence under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3731(a)(1) and (a)(4). The court affirmed the conviction and held that appellant had a reasonable expectation of privacy in his medical records, however because the police had probable cause to request the blood test, they were entitled to obtain the results without a search warrant, regardless of who actually withdrew the blood. Where an officer had probable cause to request a blood test pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3755(a), the failure to verbalize the request did not bar the officer from obtaining the results of a medical purposes blood test without a warrant. Further, as appellant was not under arrest at the time the blood test was administered, he could not claim the explicit statutory protection of a right to refuse the test under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(b).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment for appellant's conviction. The court held that the search of appellant's medical records and seizure of his blood test results was reasonable, and did not violate the Fourth Amendment, nor did appellant have a right to refuse consent to blood testing under the implied consent scheme.

Pennsylvania State Police, Bureau of Liquor Control Enforcement v. Hospitality Invs.


No. 77 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


539 Pa. 108; 650 A.2d 854; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 603


October 18, 1993, SUBMITTED  
November 3, 1994, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted May 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3267.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Entered at No. 9104-3255, Civil Division, on July 23, 1991. JUDGE BELOW: Hon. Bernard J. Avellino



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, bureau of liquor control enforcement, sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that found Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 47, § 498, which prohibited appellee licensee from advertising the price of any malt beverage, cordial, wine, or distilled liquor offered for sale, unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, bureau of liquor control enforcement, was responsible for enforcing the Pennsylvania Liquor Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 47, § 1-101 et seq. Appellant issued a citation against appellee licensee charging a violation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 47, § 498, which prohibited appellee from advertising in any manner whatsoever the price of any malt beverage, cordial, wine, or distilled liquor offered for sale in the state. The trial court found that § 498 violated the Pennsylvania Constitution because it violated the constitutional right to commercial speech. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that § 498 was reasonably related to legitimate legislative goals in the area of alcohol control and the incidental curtailment of the right of commercial speech was justified where acquisition of a liquor license was a privilege, not a right. Therefore, § 498 was a valid exercise of Pennsylvania's right to regulate the sale and consumption of alcohol.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which found that the relevant statute, which prohibited liquor advertising by appellee licensee, was unconstitutional, holding that the statute was reasonably related to legitimate alcohol control legislative goals and the curtailment of commercial speech was justified where the acquisition of a liquor license from appellant, bureau of liquor control enforcement, was a privilege, not a right.
