Commonwealth v. Thompson


No. 22 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


538 Pa. 297; 648 A.2d 315; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 494


April 6, 1994, ARGUED  
October 6, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death for First Degree Murder, and Judgments of Sentence for Robbery, Criminal Conspiracy, and Possession of an Instrument of Crime, Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section of Philadelphia County, at Nos. 3607, 3608, 3610 and 3612 April Term, 1990. JUDGE BELOW: Hon. Eugene H. Clarke, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his death sentence from the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) as of right pursuant to statute allowing for automatic review of death sentences. Defendant was convicted of murder of the first degree, robbery, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime enhanced by aggravating circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 722(4), defendant sought relief from a death sentence imposed for the convictions of murder of the first degree, robbery, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime. Defendant claimed that the court erred in a jury instruction at the penalty phase of the trial as to aggravating circumstances. The court affirmed the trial court's death sentence. The court held that the charge had been read in its entirety to the jury and that the trial court had repeatedly instructed the jury that it was required to find beyond a reasonable doubt that an aggravating circumstance had been proven. The trial court was merely reminding the jury of what it had done in the guilt phase of the trial, finding that defendant had killed the victim in the perpetration of a felony of robbery. The instruction was that the jury could find defendant guilty of robbery only if it found that he had caused serious bodily injury in the course of committing a theft. Since the jury convicted defendant of robbery, it must have found that he committed the shooting while perpetrating the theft.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's convictions and death sentence of defendant because the jury instruction on aggravating circumstances was properly given and the jury had found beyond a reasonable doubt that an aggravated circumstance existed to allow for a death sentence as opposed to a life sentence.

Commonwealth v. Brown


No. 19 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


538 Pa. 410; 648 A.2d 1177; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 496

October 6, 1994, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. Brown, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 908 (Pa., Apr. 29, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, at Nos. 3888, 3890, and 3895, July Term, 1990, dated April 8, 1992. JUDGE BELOW: Hon. Paul Ribner.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction for murder in the first degree, robbery, and possession of an instrument of crime, for which he was sentenced to death by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), after a jury found him guilty and reviewed both aggravating and mitigating circumstances before unanimously reaching a verdict of death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury for the crimes of murder in the first degree, robbery, and possession of an instrument of crime, and was sentenced to death. Appellant claimed several errors on appeal, including the ineffective assistance of his trial counsel for counsel's inactions at trial, the fact that he was shackled during trial, and the presentation of mitigating circumstances at sentencing. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence, finding that both were supported by substantial evidence and that counsel was not ineffective because appellant's proposed objection was meritless. Further, shackling was necessary and permitted under exceptional circumstances, and the evidence showed that the jury had considered all sentencing evidence, however the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating ones. Finally, the court found no abuse of the discretion of the trial court for denying a motion for a new trial, because the jury's verdict did not shock the conscience nor demand another opportunity for justice to prevail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of appellant for murder in the first degree, robbery, and possession of an instrument of crime. The court found that appellant had been given effective assistance, that the jury was within its province when it reviewed aggravating and mitigating circumstances for sentencing, and that the denial of a motion for a new trial was not a abuse of discretion because the verdict did not shock the conscience of the court.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought automatic review of judgments of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two murders, possession of an instrument of crime, and abuse of a corpse, and imposed sentences of death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the murder of two victims and sentenced to death for the murder of each victim. Defendant contended that his trial counsel was ineffective, that the death penalty statute, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, was unconstitutional, and that the trial court gave a response to a question posed by the jury during deliberations that improperly infringed upon the sole province of the jury to weigh aggravating and mitigating circumstances. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions but reversed his sentences. Therefore, the court held that defendant was prejudiced by trial counsel's failure to object and that a new hearing as to penalty was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions but reversed his sentences, holding that the trial court's instruction that the jury must consider the lack of unanimity regarding findings of mitigating circumstances when determining whether the mitigating circumstances were outweighed by the aggravating circumstances interfered with the province of the jury. Thus, the court held that defendant was prejudiced by trial counsel's failure to object.

Terminato v. Pennsylvania Nat'l Ins. Co.


No. 47 Western District Appeal Docket 1993


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


538 Pa. 60; 645 A.2d 1287; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 332


March 9, 1994, ARGUED  
August 4, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the order of the Superior Court of January 11, 1993, at No. 1489 Pgh. 1991, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County of July 23, 1991, Civil Division at No. 91-2089. 422 Pa. Super. 92, 618 A.2d 1032 (1993) 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insured challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's order that it lacked jurisdiction over a claim against appellee insurer for denial of coverage after an adverse peer review under the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1701 et seq., because appellant failed to request reconsideration of the peer review under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1797(b).


OVERVIEW: The case presented a question of first impression under the Motor Vehicle Financial Responsibility Law (MVFRL), 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1701 et seq. The MVFRL was amended by the Act of February 7, 1990, P.L. 11 (Act 6), which established a peer review plan for evaluating treatment and health care services provided to a person injured in an automobile accident. Appellant insured filed a claim for medical expenses resulting from an auto accident under her policy with appellee insurer. After an adverse peer review, appellee denied the claim. The trial court held and the appellate court affirmed that as 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1797(b) provided for a request for reconsideration of an initial peer review decision, appellant insured had not exhausted a mandatory administrative remedy. The court held peer review organizations under § 1797(b) were not administrative agencies nor should they be afforded deference as they are not neutral. The court reversed the decision below and remanded for further proceedings as it held § 1797(b) was not an alternative dispute resolution procedure and that there was no exhaustion requirement before judicial resolution of a policy dispute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's order and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings. The court held the statutory provision for reconsideration of adverse peer reviews of insurance claims based on medical treatment for injuries was not a mandatory administrative remedy that required exhaustion before a claimant could seek judicial review.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, Entered on November 1, 1991, at No. CC 9008151.

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division, Entered on November 1, 1991, at No. CC 9008122.

Appeal from the Judgment of the Superior Court, No. 03284, Philadelphia, 1991, filed July 13, 1992, Affirming Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Criminal Division, entered October 8, 1991 at 1848-90.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) which denied its motion to modify sentences of appellees two and three for possession of marijuana. The court granted allocatur on the sentencing issue after the Superior Court of Philadelphia affirmed the judgment of sentence convicting appellee one on several charges related to marijuana possession.


OVERVIEW: Appellee one was convicted of possession, possession with intent to deliver marijuana, and conspiracy. He was sentenced to three to six years' imprisonment on the possession with intent to deliver conviction pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7508(a)(1)(ii) and was fined $ 15,000. He was also sentenced to a concurrent term of imprisonment of eleven and one-half to twenty-three months for the criminal conspiracy conviction. Appellee two and three were convicted of possession of 116 pounds of marijuana with intent to deliver. The court reversed the orders of the lower courts and remanded the matters for sentencing in accordance with the court's opinion because the court found that although the legislature did not expressly provide a maximum sentence in 18 Pa. Cons. § 7508(1)(a)(ii) or (iii), one could be reasonably implied when that section is read together with 35 P.S. § 780-113(f)(2). The court found that the fact that its construction was contrary to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9756(b)'s minimum-maximum rule was of no constitutional moment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower courts regarding sentencing of appellees one, two, and three and remanded the cases for further proceedings. It found that although a maximum sentence was not expressly provided in the statutory provision for sentencing for drug trafficking it could reasonably be implied when the provision was read together with a provision of the Drug Act. Appellees were not denied due process.

Commonwealth v. Fahy

SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


537 Pa. 533; 645 A.2d 199; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 245

Appeal from the refusal of the Honorable Albert F. Sabo of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, to vacate the death penalty following a hearing held pursuant to this Court's remand Order of January 13, 1992, as of Nos. 2284-2289 of the February Term of 1981 of the Aforesaid Court dated December 8, 1992.

Appeal from the Refusal of the Honorable Albert F. Sabo of the Philadelphia Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Trial Section, to Vacate the Death Penalty Following a Hearing Held Pursuant to this Court's Remand order of January 13, 1992, as of Nos. 2284-2289 of the February Term of 1981 of the aforesaid Court Dated December 8, 1992.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), convicting him of first-degree murder, rape, burglary, possession of an instrument of crime, sentencing him to death for torturing and killing a 12-year-old girl.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder, rape, burglary, possession of an instrument of crime, and sentenced to death for torturing and eventually killing a 12-year-old girl. The court affirmed because appellant's counsel was not rendered ineffective in not requesting that the court define torture as an aggravating circumstance that required a specific intent to commit torture separate from the specific intent to commit murder. The evidence showed that the victim's final hours were marked by unspeakable horrors, inflicted to satiate appellant's sadistic appetite. Appellant's trial counsel was not ineffective for failing to assert a meritless claim.


OUTCOME: Appellant's death sentence was affirmed for the murder of a young girl because his trial counsel was not ineffective in not asking the court to define torture as an aggravating circumstance, which required a specific intent to commit torture separate from the specific intent to commit murder.

Commonwealth v. Rivers


No. 14 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


537 Pa. 394; 644 A.2d 710; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 271


October 20, 1993, ARGUED  
July 1, 1994, DECIDED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied March 18, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1825.
Writ of certiorari denied Rivers v. Pennsylvania, 516 U.S. 1175, 134 L. Ed. 2d 217, 116 S. Ct. 1270, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 1825 (1996)
Post-conviction proceeding at Commonwealth v. Rivers, 567 Pa. 239, 786 A.2d 923, 2001 Pa. LEXIS 2701 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, March Term, 1988, Information Nos. 3519, 3521 and 3523.
Commonwealth v. Rivers, 23 Phila. 242, 1991 Phila. Cty. Rptr. LEXIS 97 (Pa. C.P., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged her conviction from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section (Pennsylvania), of murder in the first degree in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502, and death sentence, asserting trial court error in admitting a photograph of the decedent alive and a photograph of appellant in a newspaper article relating the interest of the police in speaking with her regarding the death.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellant of murder in the first degree, in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2502, and related crimes, and the trial court sentenced her to death. On appeal, appellant asserted error by the trial court in admitting a photograph of the decedent alive. The court found that the existence of the decedent as a life in being was clearly established through the testimony of various witnesses. The photograph was introduced for the purpose of engendering sympathy for the decedent with the intent of creating an atmosphere of prejudice against appellant. The admission of this photograph was error. However, the testimony surrounding the photograph was limited, thus in light of the overwhelming circumstantial evidence of appellant's guilt, the court concluded that the error was harmless. Appellant further objected to a photograph of herself in a newspaper article relating the interest of the police in speaking with her regarding the death. The court held that the introduction of the article containing the photograph was relevant to the commonwealth's position that appellant was eluding the police, thus creating an inference of guilt. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and death sentence on the grounds that although admission of the photograph of the decedent was improper and irrelevant, the error was harmless in light of the overwhelming circumstantial evidence of appellant's guilt. Introduction of the newspaper article containing appellant's photograph was relevant to the commonwealth's position that appellant was eluding the police.

 

121 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Marshall


NO. 0013 CAPITAL APPEAL DOCKET


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


537 Pa. 336; 643 A.2d 1070; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 160


December 7, 1993, ARGUED  
May 24, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, at No. 1730, November Term, 1983, and Dated March 25, 1991.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which imposed a sentence of death on him after the jury found that appellant had committed murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was brought to trial on charges of criminal homicide for the deaths of two women and the two-year-old daughter of one of the women. The jury in that trial returned verdicts of murder in the first degree as to each victim. The sentence of death imposed for the death of the child was vacated and the matter was remanded to the trial court for a new sentencing hearing. The jury fixed appellant's penalty at death. Post-trial motions were considered and denied, and appellant was sentenced to death by the trial court. The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court. Photographs of the crime scene were not inflammatory, and could not have prejudiced appellant, and thus were properly admitted. An officer's testimony was not inflammatory and could not have prejudiced appellant. There was nothing in the record to support appellant's claim that the trial court inserted its personal opinions into the jury's deliberations or essentially told the jury to sentence appellant to death.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court which convicted appellant of murder in the first degree. It found that a new sentencing hearing was consistent with statute, photographs and testimony were not inflammatory or prejudicial, and the trial court did not insert its personal opinions that the jury should impose a death sentence into jury deliberations.

 

122 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Cry, Inc. v. Mill Serv.


No. 5 W.D. Appeal Docket 1993


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


536 Pa. 462; 640 A.2d 372; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 88


September 21, 1993, ARGUED  
March 29, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court, Docket No. 2506 C.D. 1991, dated October 30, 1992, Reversing the Order dated September 10, 1991 of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Civil Division, at Docket No. 3078 of 1990. 153 Pa. Comwlth. 13 619 A.2d 807 (1992).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), finding that the Department of Environmental Resources was not an indispensable party and that the appellees' environmental pollution action lied in the court of common pleas.


OVERVIEW: Appellees brought a citizens lawsuit under certain environmental statutes against appellant for allegedly polluting the environment with hazardous waste. The issue was whether the action required the joinder of the Department of Environmental Resources (DER) as an indispensable party. The court held that DER was an indispensable party defendant because justice could not be done in its absence. The concern was phrased as a balancing of the interests between appellees, appellant, the absent third-party, and the efficient administration of justice. Moreover, DER satisfied the relevant criteria for joinder as an indispensable party. Finally, the court placed the jurisdiction of the case in the commonwealth court for the sake of the public interest, and to avoid a multiplicity of lawsuits emanating from different counties.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and joined the Department of Environmental Resources as an indispensable party in appellees' action against appellant for allegedly polluting the environment with waste. The action was within the original jurisdiction of the commonwealth court.

McCusker v. Workmen's Comp. Appeal Bd. (rushton Mining Co.)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on January 28, 1992 at No. 385 C.D. 1991, affirming the order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board entered on January 31, 1991 at Docket No. A90-51. 145 Pa. Cmwlth. 261, 603 A.2d 238 (1992) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), affirming an order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board, denying appellant worker's compensation benefits because he entered into a meretricious relationship in violation of the Worker's Compensation Act.


OVERVIEW: Appellant began receiving worker's compensation after his wife was killed in a work-related accident. Appellant's employer filed a petition to terminate his benefits alleging that he was living in a meretricious relationship in violation of the Worker's Compensation Act § 307(7). The referee granted the employer's petition, and the order of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board affirming the referee's decision was subsequently affirmed by the trial court. In accord with the deferential scope of review the court was required to afford social welfare legislation, the court determined that the proper level of scrutiny was the rational relationship test. The court affirmed the order of the trial court because § 307(7) was directed at the accomplishment of a legitimate governmental interest, and done so in a manner that was not arbitrary or unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the termination of appellant's worker's compensation benefits because he lived with a woman in violation of the Worker's Compensation Act after his wife died in a work-related accident. The applicable statute survived scrutiny under the rational relationship test.

Commonwealth v. Louden


No. 61 M.D. Appeal Docket 1989, No. 62 M.D. Appeal Docket 1989


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


536 Pa. 180; 638 A.2d 953; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 50


May 7, 1991, ARGUED  
March 11, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon Co. dated September 8, 1989 at Nos. 87-10695 and 87-10696.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lebanon County (Pennsylvania) which granted appellees a new trial after tape recordings of appellees screaming at children and the sound of slapping and children crying were suppressed but videotaped testimony of children was heard by the jury in trial alleging that appellees had endangered children.


OVERVIEW: Appellees were arrested for endangering the welfare of a child, Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4304, after the husband's sister made a complaint and tape recorded their explicit sexual conversations and the sound of slapping, from the next door residence, while appellees were operating a day care center in their home. The jury convicted appellees for endangering the welfare of a child. Appellees filed a motion in arrest of judgment and for new trial. At trial the jury was shown videotaped testimony of the children made pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5985. The court reversed the order of the trial court suppressing the tape recordings but affirmed the order of the lower court granting appellees a new trial because it determined that the conversations were not "oral communications" protected under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5702. The court further found that appellees were entitled to a new trial because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §§ 5984 and 5985 failed to guarantee them the right to confront their accusers in violation of Pa. Const. art. I § 9.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order suppressing tape recordings of appellees loudly arguing and the sound of slapping because the recordings were not protected oral conversations. The court affirmed the lower court's order for a new trial for appellees because it determined that the videotaped testimony of children that was shown to the jury denied appellees their constitutional right to confront their accusers.

Commonwealth v. Nicely


No. 15 W.D. Appeal Docket 1992, No. 16 W.D. Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


536 Pa. 144; 638 A.2d 213; 1994 Pa. LEXIS 49


March 10, 1993, ARGUED  
March 10, 1994, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, dated February 11, 1992, at No. 2496 C 1990.

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, dated February 11, 1992, at No. 3658 C 1990.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee probationers relief from the payment of supervisory fees required pursuant to 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 180-7.20.


OVERVIEW: Appellee probationers were notified that they were to pay a monthly supervisory fee pursuant to 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 180-7.20. The supervisory fee became effective after appellees were placed on probation. Appellees sought relief from payment of the fees and their petitions were consolidated for review. Appellees claimed that the fee constituted an ex post facto law and violated the separation of powers doctrine. Appellees also claimed that the fee imposed greater punishment than was set forth at the time the punishable act was committed and violated their rights to due process. The trial court granted appellees' request for relief and held that imposition of the fee violated the concept of separations of powers and that § 180-7.20 was unconstitutional as applied to appellees. The prosecution appealed the trial court's order. The appellate court reversed and held that the trial court committed an error of law by holding § 180-7.20 unconstitutional. The court held that the trial court had jurisdiction to modify appellees' probation conditions in order to comply with the General Assembly's mandate that persons on probation contribute to the cost of their supervision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order that granted appellee probationers' petition for relief from the payment of supervisory fees. The court held that there was no violation of the separation of powers doctrine when appellees were notified that they were required to pay the monthly supervisory fee.

Wilkinsburg Police Officers Ass'n by & Through Harder v. Commonwealth


No. 50 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1989


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


535 Pa. 425; 636 A.2d 134; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 322; 147 L.R.R.M. 2943


May 6, 1991, ARGUED  
December 30, 1993, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered September 8, 1989, at No. 66 M.D. 1989.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officers association sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellee city in appellant's action opposing appellee's fiscal recovery plan and claiming that sections of the statute authorizing the plan were unconstitutional. The trial court concluded that it lacked jurisdiction when no claims other than contract claims remained.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city adopted a fiscal recovery plan after it was designated as financially distressed under the Financially Distressed Municipalities Act (act), 53 P.S. §§ 11701.101-11701.501. Appellant police officers brought an action claiming the plan violated provisions of its collective bargaining agreement and several sections of the act were unconstitutional. The trial court sustained several preliminary objections raised by appellee and dismissed the constitutional claims. The court held that sovereign immunity barred appellant's claims against the commonwealth, did not bar appellant's constitutional claims against appellee, but barred damages on its contract claim. The court found the act did not unconstitutionally delegate fiscal authority because appellee's governing body retained the decision-making authority under the plan. The court held that the act was rationally related to a proper commonwealth purpose. The court also held that, having stricken all counts except the contractual claim against appellee, the trial court properly concluded it lacked subject matter jurisdiction over the dispute. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order sustaining the preliminary objections of appellee city in appellant police officers association's action challenging appellee's fiscal recovery plan and challenging the constitutionality of the statute authorizing the plan. The court affirmed the trial court's determination that it lacked jurisdiction when nothing but the contract claims remained.

Plowman v. Department of Transp., Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 39 M.D. Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


535 Pa. 314; 635 A.2d 124; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 302


April 7, 1993, ARGUED  
December 14, 1993, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas for the 39th Judicial District-Franklin County Branch, entered March 31, 1992, at Docket No. Misc. Vol. AA, Page 213



DISPOSITION: Reversed and denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas for the 39th Judicial District, Franklin County (Pennsylvania), which held that 35 P.S. § 780-113(m) was unconstitutional in appellee licensee's action that challenged the suspension of her driver's permit because of her conviction for possession of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: After appellee licensee pleaded guilty to possession of marijuana, appellee's driver's permit was suspended by appellant commonwealth pursuant to 35 P.S. § 780-113(m). The lower court held that the statute was unconstitutional because it failed to accomplish a legitimate state objective. The court found that the statute was to be evaluated under a rational basis analysis because a driver's license was a privilege and not a fundamental right. The court held that the statute promoted the legitimate interest of protecting against the proliferation of drug use. The court also held that it could identify potential benefits to appellant's citizens from the statute because the loss of a driver's license could deter a potential drug user from committing the offense, since the criminal penalty for the offense might only be minor. The court reversed the judgment of trial court because both prongs of the rational basis test were met.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court because a statute allowing the suspension of appellee's driver's permit for a drug conviction was constitutional since it promoted the legitimate interest of protecting the public against increased drug use.

Jenkins v. Hospital of the Medical College


No. 23 E.D. Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


535 Pa. 252; 634 A.2d 1099; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 292


December 9, 1992, SUBMITTED  
December 8, 1993, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment entered January 31, 1991, by the Superior Court of Pennsylvania at No. 3594 Philadelphia 1988, reversing the Order dated December 6, 1988 and entered December 12, 1988 from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, No. 5561, February Term 1986. 401 Pa. Super. 604, 585 A.2d 1091 (1991) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which reversed an order of the trial court striking the portion of appellee patient's complaint for recovery of damages due to the negligence of appellant in failing to perform tests for the presence of Downs syndrome within sufficient time for her to elect to terminate her pregnancy.


OVERVIEW: Appellee patient underwent sterilization immediately after the birth of her son. Appellee sued appellant hospital to recover damages based on the alleged negligence of appellant in not informing her prior to the sterilization procedure that the child had Downs syndrome and in failing to test for the presence of the disorder within sufficient time for her to elect to terminate the pregnancy. The trial court struck the wrongful birth claims, holding those claims were precluded under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 42, § 8305, which by its own terms had retroactive effect to any case pending or under appeal. The appellate court reversed the order, holding the retroactive application of the statute unconstitutional, and remanded the case. The court held that the order was properly appealable because the complaint stated two separate and distinct causes of action and the trial court rendered final judgment on one of those. The court held that the retroactivity provision could not constitutionally be applied to appellee because her vested right in the cause of action accrued several years prior to the enactment of the statute. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order reversing the trial court's order striking the wrongful birth claims of appellee patient in her suit against appellant hospital because the statute prohibiting wrongful birth claims could not be constitutionally applied to appellee.

Commonwealth v. Edmiston


No. 30 Capital Appeal Docket


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


535 Pa. 210; 634 A.2d 1078; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 252


March 8, 1993, ARGUED  
November 10, 1993, DECIDED

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From the Order Dated March 21, 1991 of The Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County Denying Appellant's Motion for New Trial And/Or in Arrest of Judgment at Information No. 1025-88.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Cambria County (Pennsylvania) which convicted defendant of murder of the first degree, rape, statutory rape, and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to being sentenced to death for rape and murder, defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient to support the verdict and that the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence. The court first held that the prosecution disproved the asserted defense of voluntary intoxication, as witnesses testified that defendant was in control of his own actions. The court also held that evidence concerning the manner in which the victim died established that defendant possessed the specific intent to kill the victim. Reviewing the record, the court concluded that the evidence was sufficient to support the verdicts. The court then held that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence, as defendant voluntarily confessed and was not detained illegally. The court noted defendant had been free to leave the state troopers' barracks at any time prior to his confession. Additionally, the court held that the death penalty, as applied to defendant, was proportional to other sentences in similar crimes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder, rape, and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, as well as defendant's death sentence, as the evidence was sufficient to support the verdict, and the trial court did not err in the admission of evidence.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This matter came before the court for reargument after the court initially ruled that the designation of a building as historic without the consent of the owner was a taking that required just compensation.

Commonwealth v. Moore

November 9, 1993, DECIDED

Post-conviction relief granted at, in part, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Moore, 580 Pa. 279, 860 A.2d 88, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2500 (2004)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Moore v. Beard, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 91025 (M.D. Pa., Dec. 11, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of Death entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, Criminal Division, at No. 22 of 1983, and dated September 27, 1988.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his judgment of sentence of death, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, which was entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) following his conviction of murder of the first degree. Appellant asserted numerous errors that he claimed were predicated by ineffective counsel and that the death penalty statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his conviction of first-degree murder and imposition of the death sentence, pursuant to Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711, defendant claimed that his counsel was ineffective and that § 9711 was unconstitutional. In affirming the conviction and sentence, the court held that defendant's counsel was effective, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to remove appointed counsel. The court found that defendant's unsupported claims of ineffective counsel possessed no arguable merit, that he was not prejudiced by alleged errors, and that he failed to provide a reasonable basis to support remand for an evidentiary hearing. The court concluded that § 9711 was not unconstitutional, that it did not violate the separation of powers doctrine, and that it did not create a special tribunal to consider the death penalty. The court stated that the jury's imposition of the death sentence was not the product of passion, prejudice, or arbitrarily imposed. The court found that defendant's intent to rob was an aggravating circumstance that supported the death penalty, and the death sentence was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of first-degree murder and the death sentence. The court held that defendant's counsel was effective, and the trial court did not err in refusing to remove appointed counsel. The court also held that the death penalty statute was not unconstitutional, that it did not violate the separation of powers doctrine, and that it did not create a special tribunal to consider the death penalty.

Commonwealth v. Young


No. 156 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


536 Pa. 57; 637 A.2d 1313; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 250


December 9, 1992, RE-ARGUED  
November 5, 1993, DECIDED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the October 24, 1991 Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County at Information Nos. 900-87 through 900.8-87 which ordered Joseph Louis Young a/k/a Yusuf Ali to serve a Sentence of Death



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), convicting him of murder in the first degree, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree, and he was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in allowing the prosecutor to use a peremptory challenge to exclude a prospective juror who was the same race as defendant, that the testimony of witnesses establishing that defendant had a significant history of felony convictions was so irrelevant, inflammatory and prejudicial that it deprived him of a fair trial, and that it was an abuse of discretion for the trial court to refuse to admit a packet of letters written by defendant into evidence. The court ruled that defendant did not establish any facts and circumstances which raised an inference of purposeful discrimination. Further, the court held that a trial court did not err when it refused to limit evidence of defendant's prior convictions to a certified record and sanitized summary of the circumstances of the prior crimes. The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant, concluding that the trial court did not err in excluding the letters because to allow the letters into evidence would have been tantamount to granting defendant the right of allocution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant because photographs of a crime scene were admissible if they were relevant, could assist the jury in understanding the facts of the case, and did not unduly inflame the passions of the jury. The state was afforded reasonable latitude in arguing its position to the jury and could employ oratorical flare in arguing for the death penalty.

Blum v. Merrell Dow Pharmaceuticals


No. 65 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


534 Pa. 97; 626 A.2d 537; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 134


January 22, 1992, Argued  
June 3, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court, dated June 14, 1989, at Nos. 1813, 1866, 1867 & 2016 Philadelphia 1988, affirming in part and reversing in part the Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Civil Division, dated May 12, 1988, amended May 25, 1988, at No. 1027 September Term 1982. 385 Pa. Super. 151, 560 A.2d 212 (1989) 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the Order of the Superior Court which reversed the Court of Common Pleas and remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, minor and parents, challenged an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which remanded for a new trial on the grounds that appellee pharmaceutical company was denied its right to a trial by jury of 12 members under Pa. Const. art. I, § 6.


OVERVIEW: Appellee pharmaceutical company filed a written demand for a 12-member jury in an action filed by appellants, minor and parents. During trial, one juror was dismissed, and the trial proceeded with 11 jurors. The lower court reversed, holding that appellee's right to a 12-member jury under Pa. Const. art. I, § 6 was violated. The court found that U.S. Const. amend. VI did not require a 12-member jury, but that each state was entitled to provide broader standards. The court then held that the state constitution protected the right to trial by jury as it existed at common law. The court also held that the phrase "trial by jury shall be as heretofore" preserved the right to a jury trial with its substantial elements, such as the number of jurors. The court therefore affirmed because appellee was deprived of his constitutional right to a 12-member jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because appellee pharmaceutical company was denied its rights under the state constitution, which preserved the right to a jury trial as it existed at common law, with its substantial element of a 12-person jury.

Reichley by Wall v. N. Penn Sch. Dist.


No. 180 E.D. Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


533 Pa. 519; 626 A.2d 123; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 124; 144 L.R.R.M. 2848


April 6, 1992, Argued  
May 27, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County dated October 16, 1990, at No. 86-15062



DISPOSITION: The Order of the Court of Common Pleas holding Act 195 unconstitutional insofar as it allows strikes by public educators is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association, appellant teachers, and appellant employees sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Philadelphia) that held the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA) § 101 et. seq., 43 P.S. § 1101.101 et seq., was unconstitutional insofar as it allowed strikes by public educators, in an action by appellee students and parents for declaratory and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: In 1986, negotiation and mediation failed to produce a new collective bargaining agreement between appellee school district and appellant union. As permitted by the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA) § 1003, 43 P.S. § 1101.1003, appellant teachers and appellant employees represented by appellant union commenced a strike on September 2, 1986. The next month, appellees, students and parents, filed an action against appellee district, appellant union, and the commonwealth, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief on the basis that PERA was unconstitutional to the extent that it allowed teachers to strike. The trial court initially dismissed the action. On appeal, the commonwealth court held the commonwealth was not an indispensable party to the action for declaratory relief. Accordingly, the case was remanded. On remand, the trial court held PERA was unconstitutional insofar as it granted public school teachers the right to strike. Consequently, appellants sought review. On review, the court reversed because appellees, students and parents, failed to prove that the provisions of PERA, which allowed a strike, were clearly, palpably, and plainly unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that held the statute unconstitutional because appellee students and appellee parents failed in their burden of proving the statute was clearly, palpably, and plainly unconstitutional.

West Shore Sch. Dist. v. Pennsylvania Labor Relations Bd.


No. 28 M.D. Appeal Dkt. 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


534 Pa. 164; 626 A.2d 1131; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 146; 145 L.R.R.M. 2634


September 27, 1991, Argued  
May 26, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court filed on February 1, 1990, at No. 15 Misc. Dkt. 1990 Denying a Preliminary Injunction. 131 Pa. Cmwlth. 476, 570 A.2d 1354 (1990) 



DISPOSITION: The order of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin appellee board from issuing an adjudication or conducting a hearing on two unfair labor practices matters filed by appellee union against appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school district filed a petition for review in the nature of an action in equity in the commonwealth court against appellee board and appellee union in order to enjoin appellee board from holding a hearing or issuing an adjudication on two unfair labor charges brought by appellee union. In support of its request for injunctive relief, appellant school district argued that appellee board no longer existed because it had not been reestablished under the Sunset Act, 71 P.S. § 1795.1. The commonwealth court determined that appellee board was a viable state agency, and denied appellant's request for an injunction. Consequently, appellant sought review. The court affirmed the denial of appellant's request for injunctive relief, because the Sunset Act was invalid. Therefore, the limitation on the existence of appellee board as set forth in the Sunset Act was void.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied appellant school district's request for injunctive relief because the statutory limitation on the existence of appellee board was void.

Commonwealth v. Boyle


No. 88 W.D. Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


533 Pa. 360; 625 A.2d 616; 1993 Pa. LEXIS 101


March 13, 1992, Argued  
May 26, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania Dated May 2, 1990 at Docket No. 116 Pittsburgh 1989, Affirming the Judgement of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, Dated December 21, 1988 at Docket No. 1984-571. 394 Pa. Super. 513, 576 A.2d 967 (1990) 



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we vacate the judgment of sentence and remand for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which affirmed his conviction by jury on seven counts of failing to file Pennsylvania sales tax returns, for which he was sentenced to imprisonment and fines.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of failing to file Pennsylvania sales tax returns and imprisonment and fines were imposed. The Superior Court affirmed the conviction. The court found that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 268(b) provided for misdemeanor charges against a person who willfully failed or neglected to timely file a return. The court found that culpability was the key factor in imposing criminal penalties. Defendant argued that newly-discovered evidence that was not presented at trial would have shown that defendant lacked intent and was not aware of the completion of an audit, and therefore he did not willfully fail to file the returns. The court vacated defendant's conviction and remanded the case for a new trial so that a jury could consider the newly-discovered evidence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the conviction of defendant for failing to file Pennsylvania sales tax returns, and remanded for a new trial, holding that after-discovered evidence existed that a jury should have considered as to defendant's intent and his argument that he was unaware that an audit of his records had been completed.

Commonwealth v. Williams


NO. 16 E.D. APPEAL DOCKET 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


532 Pa. 265; 615 A.2d 716; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 473


April 6, 1992, Argued  
October 9, 1992, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Criminal Division, entered January 29, 1990, at Nos. 2563-2565, May Term, 1987. Hon. Michael R. Stiles.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we affirm the judgment of sentence of death. Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for first degree murder and death sentence, entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Defendant went running down a street and began shooting at a car. The victim passed between defendant and the car, and was shot in the back. Defendant then fled the scene on foot, but was identified by eyewitnesses. Defendant was arrested and charged with the victim's murder, tried by a jury, convicted of murder and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence and argued that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his conviction, that he had received ineffective assistance of counsel, and that the trial court erred in its instructions regarding mitigation factors. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence and held that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction. The court further held that defendant had not shown that counsel's decisions and strategy were unreasonable, and that he had been prejudiced by counsel's actions during the guilt or punishment phase of the trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree murder and death sentence, holding that the evidence supported the conviction and that defendant had failed to show that his counsel was ineffective.

Harristown Dev. Corp. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of General Services


Nos. 128, 130 & 137 M.D. Appeal Docket, 1990

532 Pa. 45; 614 A.2d 1128; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 448


May 5, 1992, Argued  
September 21, 1992, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Orders of Commonwealth Court dated September 14, 1990 and November 19, 1990 at No. 5 MD89. 135 Pa. Commwlth. 177, 580 A.2d 1174 (1990).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, department of general services, the commonwealth, and senator, as well as appellee nonprofit organization challenged orders of the commonwealth court (Pennsylvania), finding that 71 P.S. § 632(d) was unconstitutional, and granting appellant senator legislative immunity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant senator proposed legislation that subjected appellee nonprofit organization to provisions of The Right to Know Law, 65 P.S. § 66.1 et seq., and The Sunshine Act, 65 P.S. § 271 et seq. Appellee filed suit against appellants, challenging the constitutionality of the legislation and seeking injunctive relief. Appellant senator claimed legislative immunity from suit. The commonwealth court granted summary judgment in favor of both parties, finding the new legislation was unconstitutional, and appellant senator was insulated by legislative immunity. The court reversed the judgment in favor of appellee on the merits of the case, and vacated the order on the issue of legislative immunity. The court upheld the new legislation as constitutional, finding that appellee leased property to the commonwealth, thus, was an agency for the purposes of the new legislation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment finding proposed legislation subjecting appellee nonprofit organization to reporting laws was unconstitutional. The court held that appellee was an agency within the meaning of the act, and that the legislation was not unconstitutional as an impairment of contracts.

Commonwealth, Dep't of Envtl. Resources v. Jubelirer


Nos. 65 and 66 M.D. Appeal Dockets 1989


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 472; 614 A.2d 204; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 461


January 22, 1990, Argued  
September 16, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, dated December 7, 1989, at No. 253 Misc. Dkt. 1989. 130 Pa. Commw. 124, 567 A.2d 741 (1989) 



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Commonwealth Court is vacated, and the appeal is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the commonwealth and officials, sought review of a decision by Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which granted appellee department of environmental resource's motion for summary judgment and ordered appellee's amended regulations published.


OVERVIEW: The independent regulatory review commission (IRRC) disapproved appellee department of environmental resource's amended regulations, under the Regulatory Review Act, 71 P.S. § 745.1-745-15. The regulations were considered under the predecessor statute's provisions. Appellee tried to get the regulations published but was told that the senate's disapproval of the governor's request for approval of the regulations barred publication. The lower court granted appellee summary judgment and ordered the regulations published. Appellants, the commonwealth officials and bodies, sought review of the decision. The court vacated the decision, holding that because the new act provided a new procedure for regulations, which in the governor's opinion addressed "conditions which may threaten the public health, safety, or welfare," the permanent bar to publication could not apply to the regulations if they were authorized under the new act. The court held that the appropriate course of action was for appellee to resubmit the regulations to the IRCC under the new Act. The court held that because that was not done, the relief requested was speculative of how the new act would operate.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the lower court's decision, finding that the case was moot when the lower court heard oral argument and holding that appellee Department of Environmental Resource should have resubmitted the regulations under the New Act.

Commonwealth v. Kohl


Nos. 183 and 188 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990, No. 11 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


532 Pa. 152; 615 A.2d 308; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 463


October 22, 1991, Argued  
September 16, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From The Judgment Of The Superior Court Entered June 18, 1990, At No. 852 Philadelphia 1988, Vacating The Judgment Of Sentence Entered In The Court Of Common Pleas Of Lehigh County On March 1, 1988, In Criminal Case Nos. 896 And 300-S Of 1985. 395 Pa. Super. 73, 576 A.2d 1049 (1990).

Appeal From The Judgment Of The Superior Court Entered On June 14, 1990, At No. 1693 Philadelphia 1988, Vacating The Judgment Of Sentence Entered In The Court Of Common Pleas Of Monroe County On May 17, 1988, In Criminal Case No. 611 of 1987. 395 Pa. Super. 1, 576 A.2d 1013 (1990) 



DISPOSITION: The orders of the Superior Court vacating the judgments of sentence and granting new trials to the Appellees, Bruce A. Kohl and Sharon Louise Danforth, are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review of the order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) that reversed appellee drunk driver's criminal convictions in the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County and the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County on the basis that samples taken under the implied consent law, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1547(a)(2), were unreasonable searches in violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 8.


OVERVIEW: Appellee drunk drivers' criminal convictions from the trial courts were reversed by the appellate court because the provision of the implied consent law that had provided for collection of blood or urine samples when an officer had reason to believe that the individual was involved in an accident with a fatality or needed medical treatment (Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1547(a)(2)), was unconstitutional. Appellant commonwealth sought review, and the court affirmed and held that the purpose underlying the implied consent statute was to enable the police to obtain evidence of intoxication or drug use to be utilized in criminal proceedings. No matter how compelling the commonwealth's interest in securing evidence that a driver was operating a vehicle under the influence of alcohol or drugs it had not evinced a special need that would justify departure from the probable cause requirements of U.S. Const. amend. IV. Appellee's challenge under Pa. Const. art I, § 8 was affirmed as well because the provision had impermissibly dispensed with the probable cause requirement and was, therefore, unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of appellee drunk driver's criminal convictions because the provision of the implied consent law relating to samples obtained after an accident had not contained a provision relating probable cause to believe that appellees were under the influence of alcohol before obtaining a blood or urine sample. The court further found that the provision had violated the state constitution.

 

144 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Pennsylvania Ass'n of Rural & Small Sch. v. Casey


No. 49 M.D. Appeal Docket 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 439; 613 A.2d 1198; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 445


May 5, 1992, Submitted  
September 16, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Findings of fact/conclusions of law at, Petition dismissed by Pa. Ass'n of Rural & Small Sch. v. Ridge, 1998 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 1 (July 9, 1998)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of Commonwealth Court dated May 8, 1991 at No. 11 Misc. Dkt. 1991



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the Commonwealth Court's order denying intervention has not had the effect of denying relief which the Appellants can obtain in no other way. Therefore, under Frey's Estate and its progeny, we quash the appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, school district and students, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which denied their petition to intervene in an action by appellees, association and others, against appellees, secretary of education and governor, seeking a declaratory judgment that the public school funding formula violated the federal and state constitution. Appellants sought to intervene pursuant to Pa. R. Civ. P. 2327.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, school district and students, sought to intervene in suit brought by appellees, association and others, against appellees, secretary of education and governor, seeking a declaratory judgment that the public school funding formula violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 1 and art. III, §§ 14 and 32. The trial court denied the petition of appellants for intervention. The court quashed the appeal because the order was not appealable where appellants had another way to secure the relief sought through intervention. The court opined that the main interest of appellants was represented by the other parties to the action, Pa. R. Civ. P. 2329(2), and the outcome of the suit did not affect appellants' ability to raise the issue of disproportionate taxation in a separate action. Further, if the issue of disproportionate taxation was raised at some time later in this suit, appellants were not precluded from petitioning to intervene at that time. Finally, to the extent that appellants' views were too important to be ignored, they had the remedy of petitioning to be heard as amicus curiae.


OUTCOME: The review sought by appellants, school district and students, was quashed because the trial court's denial of intervention did not deny relief which was not obtainable in another way and the trial court did not abuse its discretion. The interest of appellants was represented adequately by appellees, association and others, and the other intervenor in the suit against appellees, secretary of education and governor.

Masloff v. Port Auth.


No. 25 W.D. Appeal Docket 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 416; 613 A.2d 1186; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 394; 141 L.R.R.M. 2045


June 10, 1992, Submitted  
July 29, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From The Decree Dated April 10, 1992, at No. 148 M.D. 1992, In The Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the adjudication and decree of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant transit union and its president challenged the adjudication and decree entered by the judge of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which permanently enjoined a work stoppage by appellant and directed appellant's authorized representatives and the port authority to engage in court-supervised negotiations until an agreement was reached.


OVERVIEW: Appellant transit union challenged the decree entered by the trial judge, which permanently enjoined a work stoppage by appellant and directed its authorized representatives, along with the port authority, to engage in court-supervised negotiations until an agreement was reached. The parties' negotiations for an agreement were initiated, but were unsuccessful. The court affirmed and stated that unless the rules of law relied upon were palpably wrong or clearly inapplicable, a grant of injunctive relief would not be reversed on appeal. The court concluded that reasonable grounds existed for such equitable relief. The Port Authority Act, 55 P.S. § 563.2, made arbitration discretionary with the parties. In the absence of a legislative directive, the soundest approach was to treat port authority employees consistently with public employees governed by the Public Employee Relations Act, 43 P.S. § 1101.101 et seq. As for appellant's argument that it was unfair to deprive its members of the right to strike without submitting the dispute to binding arbitration, appellant was not without a remedy; such remedy was with the courts of the Commonwealth, as was other public employees.


OUTCOME: The adjudication and decree of the trial court permanently enjoining a work stoppage by appellant transit union and directing it to engage in court-supervised negotiations with the port authority was affirmed. In the absence of a legislative directive, in this instance the soundest approach was to treat port authority employees consistently with public employees governed by the Public Employee Relations Act.

Local 22, Philadelphia Fire Fighters' Union, etc. v. Commonwealth


No. 16 E.D. Miscellaneous Docket 1992, No. 17 E.D. Miscellaneous Docket, 1992


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 334; 613 A.2d 522; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 383


April 7, 1992, Argued  
June 17, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Consolidated Petitions for Review


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police, firefighters, state, county and municipal unions sought a writ of quo warranto challenging the constitutionality of the Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority Act for Cities of the First Class, Act 6, 53 P.S. § 12720.101 et seq. (1991), and the legality of an agreement entered into by respondents Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority and the city of Philadelphia.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner police, fire fighters, state, county and municipal unions sought a writ of quo warranto challenging the constitutionality of the Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority Act for Cities of the First Class, Act 6 (PICA), 53 P.S. § 12720.101 et seq. (1991), and the legality of an agreement entered into by respondents Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority and the city of Philadelphia, contending that appointment of its board members violated the separation of powers under Pa. Const. arts. II, § 1, and IV, and that the financial agreement between respondents had violated the Public Employee Relations Act, 43 P.S. § 1101.101 et seq., by requiring approval of arbitration and collective bargaining agreements. The court denied the writ and found that the PICA was constitutional, and that petitioners could not have challenged the appointment of board members without sustaining an individual grievance. The court held that the intergovernmental agreement to provide emergency financing for distressed cities had provided for nothing more than to implement a statutory plan for fiscal responsibility.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and held that the constitutional challenges to appointment of board members under the Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority Act could not be challenged in quo warranto without an individual grievance, and that the intergovernmental agreement did no more than facilitate a statutory mandate that a collective bargaining agreement be in accord with an approved financial plan.

 

147 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Blouse


No. 31 M.D. Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 167; 611 A.2d 1177; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 389


March 11, 1992, Submitted  
June 17, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 618 Harrisburg 1988, dated April 17, 1989, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, Pennsylvania, dated August 16, 1988, at No. 314 S.C.A. 1987



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the order of the Superior Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court at Harrisburg (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction for operating a motor vehicle while his license was under suspension. Defendant was apprehended at a roadblock conducted pursuant to 7 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6308(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of operating a motor vehicle while his license was under suspension after being apprehended at a roadblock. The appellate courts affirmed. The issue was the constitutionality of systematic, non-discriminatory, non-arbitrary roadblocks for the purpose of insuring safety on the highways by disclosing registration, licensing and equipment violations. The court found that the balancing of interests approach was the appropriate method for determining reasonableness under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. The court noted that 27 violations were detected in the roadblock, which evidenced the advancement of the legitimate interests it was designed to serve. The court found that the public interest was not met by less intrusive means because stops based on a probable cause or reasonable suspicion standard were incapable of detecting status offenses as opposed to observable offenses. The public interest was the mass carnage that resulted from unlicensed drivers and unsafe vehicles occupying the road. Therefore, defendant's apprehension and the statute that authorized it, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6308(b), were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court upholding defendant's conviction for operating a motor vehicle while his license was under suspension was affirmed because systematic, non-discriminatory, non-arbitrary roadblocks and the statute authorizing them were constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Baker


No. 23 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1985


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 541; 614 A.2d 663; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 423


January 22, 1990, Argued  
June 17, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petition for Reargument Denied March 2, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section at Nos. 516-520, April Term, 1984, Entered on November 9, 1987.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced him to death upon his conviction for murder and associated crimes. Defendant argued that identifications and his statement should have been suppressed, that the trial court rulings restricted the trial, that his counsel was ineffective, that his juvenile record was improperly admitted and the death penalty was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and two accomplices were tried together and defendant was convicted of murder and associated crimes and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed and argued that identifications and his statement should have been suppressed, that the trial court rulings restricted the trial, that his counsel was ineffective, that his juvenile record was improperly admitted and the death penalty was unconstitutional. The court affirmed and ruled that one of the identifications was reliable and proper, and the other was defective because counsel was not present at the lineup, but it was not used at trial. The court also ruled that the several evidentiary rulings of the trial court were proper and not restrictive, that the prosecutor's closing argument was not inflammatory, and that defendant's counsel was not ineffective. The court held that use of defendant's juvenile record was permitted in the penalty phase under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6354 and was required for a knowledgeable sentencing process. The court found that the defendant's sentence of death was consistent with other death sentences under similar circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence of death. The court ruled that the defective identification was not used at trial, that the trial court's evidentiary rulings were proper and not restrictive, that defendant's counsel was not ineffective, that the juvenile record was permitted by statute to be used at the penalty phase, and that defendant's sentence was consistent with other death sentences in similar circumstances.

Commonwealth v. Zook


No. 12 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


532 Pa. 79; 615 A.2d 1; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 428


October 24, 1991, Argued  
June 17, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Zook v. Pennsylvania, 507 U.S. 974, 113 S. Ct. 1420, 122 L. Ed. 2d 789, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 2011 (1993)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Zook, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2589 (Pa., Nov. 28, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Criminal Division, entered January 13, 1990, at No. 1700 of 1985
Pennsylvania v. Zook, 493 U.S. 873, 110 S. Ct. 203, 107 L. Ed. 2d 156, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 4624 (1989)



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we sustain the convictions of first degree murder and affirm the sentences of death.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), where he was found guilty of two counts of first degree murder and was sentenced to death. Appellant asserted numerous issues starting from the trial court's denial of his motion to quash the information lodged against him to issues revolving the imposition of the sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was involved in a robbery which ended with the violent killing of two victims. Appellant was implicated by an informant and, later, by a fellow inmate. Appellant was convicted of first degree murder and was sentenced to death. Appellant sought review and asserted a multiple errors on the part of the trial court prior to, during, and subsequent to his finding of guilt. On review, the court found that the trial court's denial of a motion to quash the information was proper due to the applicable criminal rule. There was sufficient evidence to support the convictions. Reliance by the police upon the informant's information had sufficient indicia of reliability under constitutional standards. A review of the publicity in the case revealed that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying a motion for change of venue. Further, a curative instruction was sufficient to eradicate any prejudice resulting from a remark that appellant's admission was done while in jail. The court found no other reversible constitutional, evidentiary, substantive, or procedural issue. Finally, the court determined that that the death penalty was appropriate. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Appellant's first degree murder convictions and sentence of death were affirmed because the court found no error regarding the application of the criminal rules, informant information, jailhouse admission, publicity and change of venue, or any other constitutional, evidentiary, substantive, or procedural issue. The court also found that the death penalty was appropriate under the circumstances.

Commonwealth v. Eisenhart


No. 133 M.D. Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


531 Pa. 103; 611 A.2d 681; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 351


May 6, 1991, Submitted  
June 2, 1992, Decided  
June 2, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on May 9, 1990, at No. 219 Harrisburg 1989, affirming the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of York County, entered on March 13, 1989, at No. 1227 CA 1987. 402 Pa.Super. 653, 578 A.2d 36 (1990) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for driving under the influence of alcohol and driving while his blood-alcohol level exceeded the statutory limit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI). Defendant initially agreed to submit to a blood test but at the hospital he changed his mind and refused to consent to a blood test. On the advice of the district attorney, hospital personnel conducted a blood test anyway which showed an alcohol level of .293 percent. Defendant was convicted of DUI and driving while his blood alcohol level exceeded the statutory limit. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and granted defendant a new trial. The court held that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547(a) permitted blood alcohol testing unless the subject gave an affirmative showing of his refusal to consent to the test at the time the test was administered. The court held that defendant was permitted to retract his initial consent. The court held that the results of the blood alcohol test should have been suppressed and that, because of the likely effect of the test on the DUI conviction and the statutory requirement of the other charge, the court vacated the judgment of sentence on both counts and remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol and driving while his blood-alcohol level exceeded the statutory limit for a new trial, holding that the blood-alcohol test was taken after defendant had refused to consent and that the results should have been suppressed at trial.
