Commonwealth v. Jones


No. 8 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


530 Pa. 591; 610 A.2d 931; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 325


October 24, 1991, Argued  
May 21, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied August 13, 1992.
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Jones v. Love, 1996 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 7626 (E.D. Pa., June 3, 1996)
Post-conviction proceeding at Commonwealth v. Jones, 569 Pa. 229, 802 A.2d 1232, 2002 Pa. LEXIS 1302 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Direct Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of Death, and other Consecutive Sentences, imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, on December 30, 1987, on Information Nos. 714, 716, 718, 721, 723, 725, 727, and 730, September Term, 1982. Honorable Robert A. Latrone.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), where he was convicted of two counts of murder of the first degree, six counts of aggravated assault, one count of criminal conspiracy, and one count of possession of an instrument of crime and was sentenced to death. Appellant asserted pre-trial, trial, and sentencing errors.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and codefendants were involved in a shooting spree which killed and injured bystanders. Appellant was charged and convicted of murder of the first degree, aggravated assault, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime. Appellant and was sentenced to death. Appellant sought review and asserted several pre-trial, trial, and sentencing errors. On appeal, the court found that a motion to sever appellant's trial from that of his codefendants was properly denied. As to the trial, the court determined that juror "pranks" and horseplay did not mean that the involved or other jurors were incompetent to render a fair and impartial determination of his guilt or innocence. Appellant's assertion that he had no specific intent to kill was dismissed due to the doctrine of transferred intent. The court also rejected the contention that prosecutor comments or case presentation denied appellant a fair trial after a review of the record. Nor did the court agree that appellant's trial counsel was ineffective. The court also rejected appellant's issues pertaining to the constitutionality of the death penalty statute and his sentence. The judgment was therefore affirmed.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions for murder of the first degree, aggravated assault, criminal conspiracy, and possession of an instrument of crime and his death sentence were affirmed because there were no reversible issues with regard to the motion to sever, alleged juror misconduct, mens rea assertion, prosecutor comments, or otherwise. Also, appellant's issues pertaining to the constitutionality of the death penalty statute were without merit.

 

152 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. MacElree v. Legree


No. 13 E.D. Appeal Docket 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


530 Pa. 381; 609 A.2d 155; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 323


December 4, 1991, Argued  
May 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied June 25, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order granting defendant's motion for Judgment on the Pleadings by the Honorable J. Curtis Joyner of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County entered on December 7, 1990 at No. 90-00169



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant district attorney sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Chester County (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee councilman's motion for judgment on the pleadings and determining that appellee could simultaneously hold office as both a councilman and a constable.


OVERVIEW: When appellee councilman was elected to office, he already held the elected position of constable. After refusing to relinquish the office of constable, appellant district attorney filed a quo warranto action challenging appellee's authority to hold the office of councilman. Appellant sought review after the lower court found that appellee could hold both positions pursuant to 65 P.S. §§ 410, 412. The court found that § 410 permitted a constable to be an officer in a political party, hold the office of county or state or national committee member in a political party, run for and hold elective office, and participate in any election day activities. Relying on the statutory construction rule that a general expression used in a statute was limited to things similar to items specifically enumerated in the language preceding the general expression, the court concluded that "run for and hold elective office" in § 410 meant elective offices in political parties. The court found that 65 P.S. § 14, 53 P.S. § 36001, and Coatesville, Pa., Home Rule Charter § 203 specifically prohibited a council member from holding any other elected public office. The order was reversed.


OUTCOME: The order granting appellee councilman's motion for judgment on the pleadings and determining that appellee was entitled to simultaneously hold the offices of councilman and constable was reversed. The statute relied upon by the lower court only allowed an elected official to simultaneously hold an elected office in a political party, and other statutes expressly prohibited appellee from simultaneously holding office.

Glancey v. Commonwealth State Employes' Ret. Bd.


No. 146 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1989, No. 37 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990, No. 43 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990, No. 15 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


530 Pa. 481; 610 A.2d 15; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 312


October 25, 1990, Argued; October 26, 1990, Argued  
May 18, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from Orders of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania dated June 12, December 1, and October 31, 1989 at Nos. 2018, 2026 and 2109 C.D. 1988, respectively, affirming Orders of the State Employes' Retirement Board dated July 20, 1988. 126 Pa. Commw. 457, 560 A.2d 263 (1989). 129 Pa. Commw. 290, 565 A.2d 512 (1989). 129 Pa. Commw. 444, 566 A.2d 323 (1989).

Appeal from an Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania dated July 28, 1989, at No. 2602 C.D. 1988, Affirming the Order of the State Employes' Retirement Board dated October 13, 1988. 128 Pa. Commw. 39, 562 A.2d 969 (1989) 



DISPOSITION: For all of the reasons set forth above, the judgments of the Commonwealth Court in the above cases are hereby REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former judges challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the denial of their claim for retirement benefits by appellee State Employee's Retirement Board.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former judges were all removed from office for disciplinary reasons. When the judges separately sought to collect their retirement benefits, appellee state retirement board denied their claims. Appellee contended that appellants automatically forfeited their pension benefits due to their misconduct by virtue of Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b). Each case was affirmed on appeal by the commonwealth court. Appellants challenged the judgments, and the cases were consolidated. The court reversed the denial of appellants' benefits, holding that neither the history nor the language of the state constitution supported the conclusion that Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) was meant to create broad pension forfeiture. Mere removal from the bench did automatically result in the loss of retirement benefits. The court held that the Public Employee Pension Forfeiture Act of 1978, 43 P.S. § 1311 et seq., established a specific method for the forfeiture of pensions by public officials, where certain crimes had been committed. The statute was applicable to judges and appellee could not deny benefits unless the appellants' misconduct was enumerated explicitly therein.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court affirming appellee state retirement board's denial of appellants former judges' claim for retirement benefits. The state constitution did not mandate automatic forfeiture of retirement benefits for judges removed from office. Forfeiture of benefits of public employee pensions was governed by state statute, which was applicable only to explicitly enumerated crimes.

In re 1991 Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Com.


Nos. 190, 191, 194, 196, 197, 199, 200, and 201 E.D. Misc. Docket 1991, Nos. 49, 50, 51, 52, 53, 54, 56, and 57 M.D. Misc. Docket 1991, Nos. 124, 132, 133, 134, 135, 136, 137, 138, and 141 W.D. Misc. Docket 1991


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


530 Pa. 335; 609 A.2d 132; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 279


January 25, 1992, Argued  
May 1, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Opinion May 1, 1992. Reargument Denied June 29, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Order February 14, 1992.

Appeals of the 1991 Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission's Final Plan


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens challenged the 1991 Final Plan devised by respondent Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission and adopted on November 15, 1991.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner citizens challenged a reapportionment plan written by respondent commission, which altered the legislative and voting districts within the state. The court approved the plan, noting that it was restricted in its review to determining whether the plan was in conformance with the state and federal constitutions and the Voting Rights Act, 42 U.S.C.S § 1971, et seq. The court denied petitioners' request to declare an effective date of the plan after the November 1992 election. Pa. Const. art. II § 17 clearly established the effective date of a reapportionment plan. The court held that there was no requirement that districts be redrawn in such a manner that incumbent senators remained residents of their redrawn districts. Legislators, whose offices were eliminated by the plan, did not have a constitutionally protected interest in their political seats, which were not the private domain of the officeholder. Any vacancies occurring as a result of the plan were to be filled by a special election. The court found that petitioners' allegations of gerrymandering were without merit and that the plan was consistent with the intent of the Voting Rights Act.


OUTCOME: The court ruled that the final plan of respondent commission complied with all constitutional and statutory requirements and that it was to be used for elections to the General Assembly until the next reapportionment was constitutionally required. The court noted that legislators did not have a private nor constitutionally protected interest in their offices. The court held that there was no evidence of gerrymandering.

Commonwealth v. Wharton


No. 114 E.D. Appeal Docket 1986


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


530 Pa. 127; 607 A.2d 710; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 276


December 7, 1987, Argued; January 18, 1989, Re-Argued; April 8, 1991, Re-Argued  
April 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Re-argued January 18, 1989 and April 8, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of Death Imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County on September 24, 1986 at Nos. 2258, 2260, 2261, 2262 and 2264 February Term, 1984



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction for two counts of murder and related offenses and his sentence to death on both counts from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania). Appellant claimed error on numerous grounds, including that the trial court's failure to instruct on the issue of aggravating circumstances pertaining to torture was prejudicial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction of two counts of murder in the first degree and numerous related offenses. He was sentenced to death on both counts of murder. The court affirmed the conviction. Appellant was not prejudiced by the introduction of his codefendant's confession in the joint trial. The trial court properly instructed the jury as to the use of the confession by the co-defendant. The trial court did not err in denying appellant's motion for mistrial following testimony by a witness for the state because the jury was given a curative instruction and because the remark was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Photographs of the victims and their child were admissible to demonstrate malice, an essential element of murder of the first and third degrees, and to show the history and natural development of the facts. The court held that the evidence supported two aggravating circumstances found by the jury beyond a reasonable doubt, but found that the absence of any instruction on the aggravating circumstance pertaining to torture was prejudicially deficient. The court sustained the convictions but vacated the sentences of death and remanded for a new sentence hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for two counts of murder and other offenses, but vacated the sentences of death and remanded for a new sentence hearing because there was no support for the aggravating circumstance of torture, where the trial court failed to give proper instructions. The court held that other issues of error raised lacked merit.

H.R. Miller Co. v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 31 E.D. Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


529 Pa. 478; 605 A.2d 321; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 252


December 3, 1990, Argued  
March 27, 1992, Decided  
March 27, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at Nos. 1771 and 1811 C.D. 1988, dated June 6, 1989, Affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County No. 3990 of 1987, dated June 23, 1988


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company disputed an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of a trial court, which ruled that a setback requirement, Lancaster Township, Pa., Revised Zoning Ordinance art. XIII, § 1302(2)(c) (1972), acted as an unconstitutional de facto exclusion of quarries, but which denied site-specific relief to appellant under 53 P.S. § 11011(2). The issue was whether appellant was entitled to site-specific relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellant company disputed an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order of a trial court, which ruled that a setback requirement, Lancaster Township, Pa., Revised Zoning Ordinance art. XIII, § 1302(2)(c) (1972), acted as an unconstitutional de facto exclusion of quarries, but which denied site-specific relief to appellant under 53 P.S. § 11011(2). On appeal, the court noted that the issue was the constitutionality of the ordinance, i.e., whether the ordinance was a proper exercise of the police power, and not whether appellant deserved to be able to make a particular use of his property. The court held that the purpose of a severability provision in the zoning ordinance was to salvage the ordinance if any part of it was declared invalid. The ordinance could be saved from unconstitutionality by severance of the setback provision. It was then economically and geographically feasible to locate a quarry in the industrial district. The only limitation on appellant's use derived from state regulations. Because the ordinance in that form was not exclusionary, there was no basis for ordering the site-specific relief sought by appellant. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The court held that the zoning ordinance could be saved from unconstitutionality by severance of the setback provision. It then became geographically and economically feasible to locate a quarry in the industrial district. The only limitation on appellant's use derived from state regulations. Because the ordinance in that form was not exclusionary, there was no basis for ordering the site-specific relief sought by appellant.

Appellants sought to declare the existing congressional apportionment law unconstitutional, to enjoin the implementation of the official congressional election schedule, and to adopt a valid reapportionment plan.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state senators brought issue regarding the redistricting of Pennsylvania's seats in the United States House of Representatives. Appellants filed an action against appellee state election officials regarding the existing congressional apportionment law and sought to declare the existing congressional apportionment law unconstitutional, to enjoin the implementation of the official congressional election schedule until a valid plan could be adopted, and to adopt a valid reapportionment plan if the Legislature was unable to do so. The court examined constitutional principles and precedent and ultimately approved one of appellees' plans. The approved plan had a low maximum deviation, was consistent with minimal splitting of precincts, achieved an enlarged number of congressional districts with a majority African-American population, and came closest to implementing community-of-interest factors.

The court noted that districts were meant to be equal in population to the greatest practical extent.

Pa. Public Util. Com v. Bell Tel. Co.


No. 201 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990, No. 202 Eastern District Appeal Docket 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1992 Pa. LEXIS 242; 130 P.U.R.4th 280


October 24, 1991, Argued  
March 18, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Appeal from the Orders of the Commonwealth Court Entered May 30, 1990 and July 18, 1990 at Nos. 2268, 2270, 2324 and 2371 C.D. 1989, Reversing the Order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission at Commission Docket Nos. R-891200 Through R-891200C006, Dated November 9, 1989.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the decision of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant telephone company challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the order of the state's public utility commission and ruled that a Caller ID service could not be offered in Pennsylvania, except for use by emergency service providers. The court found the service violated the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 P. S. §§ 5701-5781. Petitions for allowance of appeal were filed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant telephone company proposed to offer Caller ID. The trial court found, and the court agreed, that such service violated the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, 18 P. S. §§ 5701-5781, and could not be offered. The Caller ID terminal attached to the subscriber's telephone line and captured the incoming impulses which identified the originating number. Caller ID, therefore, fell within the general prohibition of 18 P. S. § 5771(a) and could not have been used by anyone without first obtaining a court order. At issue was whether it fell within one of the exceptions to the general prohibition. It was argued that Caller ID was allowed under the exception which permitted the use of a trap and trace device by a provider of electronic or wire communication service with the consent of the user of the service. However, the Caller ID subscriber was not an electronic or communicating service provider. The exception was only applicable to appellant, not to the subscriber. The consent provision must had to be read in light of the overall requirement of the controlling law and consent was required from all parties.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decision to prohibit Caller ID service was affirmed, where the court found that it fell within a general prohibition of the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act, and no exception applied. The court did not need to reach the constitutional issues, because the case could properly be decided on non-constitutional grounds.

OVERVIEW: Appellant telephone company proposed a tariff that offered services including a "Caller ID" service to telephone subscribers. The service was challenged by appellee consumer advocate and others. The commonwealth court determined that the proposed system was a violation of the right of privacy of the caller to a system with "Caller ID." The court determined that since the issue could be resolved on the basis of the statute, there was no basis to consider constitutional issues.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination that the "Caller ID" service proposed by appellant telephone company violated the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Act because the service was a trap and trace system that could be used by other than emergency agencies with consent of both parties to the communication.

OVERVIEW: Appellant joined forces with two others to rob a bar. Appellant killed two patrons during the robbery. He alleged that the others had previously committed a string of robberies together. Appellant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his motion for a severance of trials. The court held that appellant and his co-defendant were appropriately tried together because they were charged with the same crimes and with the same evidence, both of which stemmed from the same criminal transaction. The court affirmed.

Appellant's claimed error by the trial court in limiting testimony amounted to mere fingerpointing at his codefendant, which was not admissible. Appellant failed to establish that the jury was in any way confused about the definition of preponderance of the evidence in the sentencing stage of the trial.

Commonwealth v. Wilson


No. 21 W.D. Appeal Docket 1990, Nos. 41 & 44 E.D. Appeal Dockets 1990


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


529 Pa. 268; 602 A.2d 1290; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 35


December 6, 1990, Argued  
January 31, 1992, Decided  
January 31, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Application for Reargument Denied December 17, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the order of the Superior Court entered on June 20, 1988, at No. 481 Pittsburgh 1987, vacating judgment of sentence entered March 23, 1987, and remanding to the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, Criminal Division, at No. 494 Criminal 1986.

375 Pa. Super. 580, 544 A.2d 138 (1988).

Appeals from the order of the Superior Court entered on August 21, 1989, at No. 1781 Philadelphia 1988, reversing the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal Division, entered on January 21, 1988, at No. 1016, 1987.

387 Pa. Super. 113, 563 A.2d 1210 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, appellant state sought review of the decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed a lower court's determination that the files in a rape crisis center were protected by a statutory privilege under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5945.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state argued that the intermediate appellate court erred when it held that victim case files produced in conjunction with rape crisis counseling were not privileged, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5945.1, and were open to examination by appellee prisoners. The court stated that the legislature's enactment of § 5945.1 was intended to reverse the effect of a prior holding of the court by preventing the acquisition of all confidential communications by the victim whether in the form of live testimony or through the production of records or other documents. The court acknowledged that § 5945.1 provided the same confidentiality to clients of a rape crisis center as would have existed for a victim who sought private psychotherapeutic treatment. The court held that the privilege codified at § 5945.1 was intended to be absolute and applied to the production of the documents therein described as well as to the testimony of counselors as explicitly stated. The court also held that § 5945.1 did not impinge on appellees' right to confrontation because both appellees were given wide latitude in examining the witnesses.


OUTCOME: The orders of the intermediate appellate court that held that records produced in conjunction with rape crisis counseling were not privileged was reversed, because the legislature intended the privilege to be absolute and applied to both the production of documents and the testimony of counselors.

Commonwealth v. Kunish


No. 62 Western District Appeal Docket, 1989


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


529 Pa. 206; 602 A.2d 849; 1992 Pa. LEXIS 28


September 25, 1990, Argued  
January 28, 1992, Decided  
January 28, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court entered on January 9, 1987, at No. 799 Pittsburgh 1986, modifying the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, Criminal Division, entered April 29, 1986, at No. 634 C 1982.

363 Pa.Super. 639, 522 A.2d 661 (1987) 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed from the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) modifying the judgment of sentence of the common pleas court. The question on appeal was whether the double jeopardy clause was violated when, after sentencing the appellee to a term of incarceration, the sentencing court recalled appellee and resentenced him for the same criminal offense to a different term of incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted of voluntary manslaughter in the trial court. After sentencing appellee to a term of incarceration, the sentencing court recalled appellee and resentenced him for the same criminal offense to a different term of incarceration. Review was sought. The reviewing court left appellee with a reduced sentence even though the sentencing judge admitted error because it found double jeopardy applied to the receipt of sentences. The commonwealth sought further review. The primary question on appeal was whether the double jeopardy clause was violated when appellee was resentenced for the same criminal offense multiple times. The court noted the double jeopardy clause preserved the finality of judgments and prevented the state from making repeated attempts to convict an individual for an alleged offense. However, it also afforded criminal defendants an expectation of finality in their sentencing. The court also adopted principles from United States Supreme Court precedents that showed sentencing changes and resentencings were allowable and not violative of double jeopardy rights. The court reversed the lower court order and reinstated the trial court's sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court order and reinstated the trial court sentence appellee initially received for his conviction for voluntary manslaughter. The court stated the lower court's modification of appellee's sentence was based upon a decision that had been overruled. The court also concluded that though defendants had a right to finality in sentencing, it did not mean resentencing violated double jeopardy.

In re Act 147 of 1990


No. 229 Eastern District Miscellaneous Docket 1990


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 460; 598 A.2d 985; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 236


April 11, 1991, Argued  
November 6, 1991, Decided  
November 6, 1991, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court administrator invoked the original jurisdiction of the supreme court (Pennsylvania), pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, §§ 2 and 10, involving separation of powers, and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 721(3), involving quo warranto. She sought a declaratory judgment challenging Act 147 of 1990, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2941 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The court administrator invoked the original jurisdiction of the supreme court, pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, §§ 2 and 10, involving separation of powers, and 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 721(3), involving quo warranto. She sought a declaratory judgment challenging Act 147 of 1990 (act), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2941 et seq. The court declared the act to be invalid and unenforceable. Original jurisdiction in the supreme court for actions in quo warranto was based on 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 721(3). The act provided for the supervision, training, and certification of constables and deputy constables engaged in "judicial duties" by the court through its administrative office. This was a violation of the separation of powers. The court held that a constable is a peace officer, and as such belongs to the executive branch of government. The legislation placed constables within the judicial branch, and interfered with the supervisory authority of the supreme court just as much as attempting to dictate how that authority was to be exercised over personnel who are properly part of the judicial system. The act was unconstitutional, and as such, was invalid and unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The court held that a constable is a peace officer, and as such belongs to the executive branch of government. The legislation placed constables within the judicial branch, and interfered with the supervisory authority of the supreme court.

Commonwealth v. Chambers


No. 14 Middle District Appeal Docket 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 558; 599 A.2d 630; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 238


May 7, 1990, Argued  
November 4, 1991, Decided  
November 4, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied January 7, 1992, Reported at 1992 Pa. LEXIS 22.
Rehearing denied by Commonwealth v. Chambers, 1992 Pa. LEXIS 22 (Pa., Jan. 7, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Pennsylvania v. Chambers, 504 U.S. 946, 112 S. Ct. 2290, 119 L. Ed. 2d 214, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 3233 (1992)
Subsequent appeal at Commonwealth v. Chambers, 546 Pa. 370, 685 A.2d 96, 1996 Pa. LEXIS 2299 (1996)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Chambers, 570 Pa. 3, 807 A.2d 872, 2002 Pa. LEXIS 2013 (2002)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by Chambers v. Beard, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 58091 (M.D. Pa., Aug. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Imposed in the Court of Common Pleas of York County at No. 42 Criminal Action 1987 on February 13, 1989.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the death sentence handed down by the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania) following his convictions for the offenses of murder of the first-degree and robbery. Among multiple claims of error, appellant claimed that the prosecutor overstepped the permissible bounds of oratorical flair in his closing statements in the penalty stage of the trial by referring to the Bible.


OVERVIEW: In his closing statement of the penalty stage of appellant's trial, the prosecutors argued that the Bible said, "the murderer shall be put to death." The court noted that in the past courts have narrowly tolerated references to the Bible and have characterized such references as on the limits of "oratorical flair" and have cautioned that such references are a dangerous practice to be strongly discouraged. The court found that the argument advocated to the jury that an independent source of law existed for the conclusion that the death penalty was the appropriate punishment for appellant and that because the courts were not ecclesiastical courts there was no reason to refer to religious rules or commandments to support the imposition of a death penalty. Because the prosecutor's argument in favor of the death penalty reached outside of the evidence of the case and the law of the commonwealth, the court held that the death penalty may have been the product of passion, prejudice, or an arbitrary factor and vacated the sentence of death and remanded the matter for a new sentencing hearing under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9711(h)(4).


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions for murder of the first-degree and robbery were affirmed, but the death sentence was vacated and remanded for a new sentencing hearing because the prosecutor made prejudicial references to the Bible in his closing statements that could not be cured.

Barnes v. Barnes


No. 108 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 258; 597 A.2d 89; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 206


March 5, 1991, Argued  
October 2, 1991, Decided  
October 2, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania Dated May 30, 1989 at No. 2445 C.D. 1988 Affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County at F.D. No. 83-9390 (Family Division) entered on September 16, 1988. 126 Pa. Cmwlth. 330, 559 A.2d 980 (1989).



DISPOSITION: The order of the Commonwealth Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Department of Public Welfare, challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, (Pennsylvania) denying appellant's motion to compel the prothonotary to enter judgment on the docket against appellee for arrearages under a support order.


OVERVIEW: Appellant public welfare department (PWD) was assigned appellant mother's right to support because she was receiving public assistance. Appellant challenged lower court orders on appeal contending that denial of its motion to compel the prothonotary to enter judgment on the docket against appellee father for arrearages under a support order was improper. The court granted allocatur limited to the question of what notice was required to be given to the payor of a support obligation prior to entry of judgment on arrears under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4352. In this case, appellee was not notified as to a change in his support obligation. The court agreed with the lower courts that to permit a judgment by operation of law with regard to alleged arrearages existing as of the modified child support law without notice and a reasonable opportunity to act would be an unconstitutional deprivation of property without due process of law. By promulgating the notice provisions of §4352(g), the court concluded the legislature evinced its concern and intent to give notice to existing obligors under current orders prior to the implementation of the new provisions regarding modification of arrearages.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court orders as to arrearages occurring prior to amendment of the child support obligation statute and stated that appellee father was entitled to seek retroactive modification of his arrearages for the period based upon his alleged unemployment. After resolution of that matter on remand, the court stated that appellant public welfare department was able to proceed for a judgment, if appropriate.

The court also noted that appellee would have sustained immediate and irreparable harm as a result of the appellant city's enforcement of its new ordinance that affected some retirement benefits of appellees' members.

Automobile Trade Ass'n v. Philadelphia

Supreme Court of Pennsylvania

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Resubmitted February 13, 1991. 1


1 In addition to the original briefs and the briefs filed upon resubmission of this matter following the Court's Order of February 13, 1991, we have given due consideration to all supplemental briefs and other post-submission communications filed by the parties.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court dated September 27, 1987, at No. 3401 C.D. 1985, affirming the Orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County dated November 21, 1985, at No. 2130 November Term, 1982; 109 Pa. Cmwlth. 524, 531 A.2d 573 (1987).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, automobile dealerships and their trade association, sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee city. In the underlying suit, appellants sought refunds of taxes paid, and a declaratory judgment that a mercantile license tax was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, automobile dealerships and a trade association, filed suit seeking a declaratory judgment and refunds of taxes paid, asserting that a mercantile license tax was unconstitutional for failure to meet the uniformity requirement of Pa. Const. art. 8, § 1. When the tax was repealed, the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of appellee city on the claim for declaratory relief on grounds of mootness and on the claim for refunds. When the decision was affirmed on appeal, appellants were granted allocatur by the supreme court. The court found that the lower courts failed to undertake a proper analysis of the question whether a decision regarding the constitutionality of the tax statute would be applied retroactively and relied instead on a conclusory assertion that requiring refunds would impose a substantial hardship on appellee as the basis for denying retroactive application. The court held that appellants were not given an opportunity to present their evidence regarding the constitutionality of the statute. The court affirmed the summary judgment on the claim for declaratory relief but reversed the summary judgment on the claims for refunds and remanded the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of appellee city on the claim for declaratory relief but reversed the summary judgment on the claims for refunds because it found that appellants, automobile dealerships and a trade association, were not given an opportunity to present their evidence regarding the constitutionality of the statute under which the refunds were sought. The court remanded for further proceedings.

Commonwealth v. Tilley


No. 165 E.D. Appeal Docket 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 125; 595 A.2d 575; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 158


December 3, 1990, Argued  
July 18, 1991, Decided  
July 18, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied October 10, 1991, Reported at 1991 Pa. LEXIS 158.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of sentence of death imposed in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia at No. 1078, December Term, 1985, dated November 24, 1987.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his sentence of death imposed in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) following his conviction of murder in the first-degree.


OVERVIEW: Appellant murdered a police officer who was a member of a drug enforcement squad in the officer's home. Appellant was convicted and received the death penalty. Among his many claims of error, appellant claimed that his speedy trial rights were violated under Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100 because he was not brought to trial until 695 days after his arrest. The court held that the evidence established that the commonwealth was prepared to proceed at all times and that appellant caused virtually all of the trial delay through numerous continuances, unavailability of counsel, and changes in counsel. The court found that the trial delay actually caused by the trial court was minimal, and the commonwealth was clearly diligent in its efforts to bring appellant to trial. The court also noted that appellant did not request dismissal based upon an alleged speedy trial violation until nearly a year after the original run date. Under these facts, the court found that appellant suffered no prejudice. The court affirmed, holding held that the evidence supported the jury's finding of the aggravating circumstance and that the sentence was proportionate in comparison to sentences imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: Appellant's death sentence was affirmed because the evidence was sufficient to establish that appellant murdered his victim while perpetrating a robbery and a burglary, and supported the jury's finding of the aggravating circumstance. Appellant's speedy trial rights were not infringed because he was responsible for 19 of the 22 months of delay between his arrest and his trial, and the commonwealth was ready to proceed at all times.

Commonwealth v. Faulkner


No. 89 E.D. Appeal Dkt. 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 57; 595 A.2d 28; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 155


October 25, 1990, Argued  
July 16, 1991, Decided  
July 16, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence dated July 5, 1989 in the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, No. 88-3162, 3162.1-88 through 3162.11-88.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of sentence of death imposed by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) following his conviction of two counts of first-degree murder, rape, involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, two counts of attempted murder, criminal intent and possession of instruments of crime.


OVERVIEW: Among appellant's multiple claims of error in his capital murder trial were his claims that the trial court erred in refusing to let him introduce expert psychiatric testimony during the guilt phase of the trial. The court affirmed, holding that testimony from psychiatric experts was relevant only if it could establish that the defendant was insane under the "M'Naghten" standard, or to negate specific intent to commit first-degree murder. Both experts' reports indicated that appellant was probably psychotic and may have been delusional at the time of the murders, but that he knew the nature and quality of his acts and he understood that what he was doing was wrong. The reports also indicated that appellant had the ability to form specific intent at the time of the murder, so there was no indication that he would have a diminished capacity to from the specific intent to murder. Thus, the court held that the testimony was not relevant and was properly excluded. The court also held that testimony tending to show that appellant was "guilty but mentally ill" could only be properly admitted at the penalty phase, not the guilt phase, and was properly excluded.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions and judgment of sentence of death were affirmed because appellant's claims of insanity and diminished capacity were negated by appellant's experts' reports, which established that he could form specific intent and that he knew what he was doing was wrong. Evidence that appellant was "guilty but mentally ill" was not admissible in the penalty stage of the trial.

In re Condemnation by the Urban Redevelopment Auth.


No. 45 W.D. Appeal Docket 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


527 Pa. 550; 594 A.2d 1375; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 145


September 24, 1990, Argued; January 11, 1991, Resubmitted  
July 12, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court, entered on July 8, 1988, at No. 821 C.D. 1986, affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Civil Division, entered on February 21, 1986, at G.D. No. 81-27642; 117 Pa. Commw. Ct. 475, 544 A.2d 87 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Condemnees challenged a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), in an action in eminent domain, where the reviewing court upheld the judgment from the trial court overruling objections from condemnees to a declaration of taking by the urban redevelopment authority.


OVERVIEW: Condemnees filed preliminary objections to a declaration of taking filed by the urban redevelopment authority. The trial court overruled the objections, and the commonwealth court affirmed. Condemnees contended that they had been unconstitutionally denied a hearing to challenge the certification of blight, and that the taking was invalid for failure of the certification of blight process to comply with the requirements of the local agency law. The court affirmed. The planning commission was not required to notify property owners and hold hearings before determining that an area was appropriate for redevelopment under the Urban Redevelopment Law, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1701 et seq. The decision of the commission to designate an area for redevelopment was one that did not itself affect property rights. It was an internal decision made by a government body presumed to perform its duties in good faith and according to law. The court held that planning commissions are not required to conduct their decision-making process according to an adversarial model, bearing a burden of "proving" that their proposed actions conform to the power delegated to them by their enabling legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the commonwealth court, in an action in eminent domain, where the reviewing court upheld the judgment from the trial court overruling objections from condemnees to a declaration of taking by the urban redevelopment authority because the urban redevelopment authority was not required, under due process, to provide notice or hearing before beginning the process of designating an area for redevelopment.

United Artists Theater Circuit v. City of Philadelphia


No. 48 E. D. Appeal Docket 1990


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


528 Pa. 12; 595 A.2d 6; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 152


December 6, 1990, Argued  
July 10, 1991, Decided  
July 10, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Granted August 30, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the order of the Commonwealth Court dated May 2, 1989 at No. 1277 C.D. 1987 and No. 1985 C.D. 1987, affirming the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, No. 3955 April Term 1987, dated May 26, 1987, dismissing the appeal at No. 1277 C.D. 1987 and quashing the appeal at No. 1985 C.D. 1987.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the owner of a building housing a theater, sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the dismissal of appellant's action challenging the authority of appellee historical commission to designate appellant's theatre as an historic building.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the owner of a building housing an art deco-designed theater, argued that reviewing courts erred in upholding appellee historical commission's decision designating appellant's theater as historic. The court agreed, noting first that appellee, in invoking a city code provision to declare the building as historic over appellant's objection, placed the total financial responsibility for preserving the theater upon appellant. Explaining, the court cited due process problems with the process used by appellee to make its decision. Pointing to the lack of a neutral and detached arbiter, the court observed that appellee members not only argued the case in favor of historic designation, but also made the ultimate decision on whether the property would be so designated. Moreover, Pa. Const. art. I, § 27, encouraging the preservation of historic resources for the benefit of the people, was circumvented by the city code provision that gave absolute control over the property to appellee, but imposed all preservation costs on appellant. Thus, appellant's property was taken for public use without satisfying the constitution's just compensation requirement, the court concluded.


OUTCOME: Order dismissing appellant theater owner's suit challenging the authority of appellee historical commission to designate appellant's art deco-designed theater as historic reversed, as the "taking" of the building for public use without making just compensation was constitutionally deficient when all preservation costs of the taking were imposed on appellant, while absolute control over the property was given to appellee.

In re Blake


No. 73 E.D. Misc. Dkt. 1991


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


527 Pa. 456; 593 A.2d 1267; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 139



June 27, 1991 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the president judge of a Philadelphia, Pennsylvania lower court, sought relief from an order of the Supreme Court (Pennsylvania) that appointed two justices to oversee the reformation of the lower court and from a directive by one of the justices requiring petitioner to terminate 59 of his 65 employees.


OVERVIEW: Due to serious fiscal and administrative problems in the courts of Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, the state supreme court appointed two justices to oversee the budgetary restructuring and administrative reformation of the courts. One of the justices directed petitioner, the president judge of the lower court, to terminate 59 of the 65 staff employees attached to his office. Petitioner asked the court to set aside the order appointing the two justices and to repudiate the directive requiring the discharge of the 59 members of the court's staff. The court held that it had the authority to exercise general supervisory and administrative authority over all the courts in the state pursuant to Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(a). In addition, the court held it had the power to alter the duties of president judges pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 325(e). The court therefore held that the challenged actions were consistent with the authority of the court and entered an order denying the petition.


OUTCOME: The court entered an order denying the petition to set aside a prior order appointing two justices to oversee the reformation of the lower court and to repudiate a directive issued by one of the justices. The court held that the order and the directive were consistent with the authority vested in the court by the constitution and by statute.

Docket 1987. 127 Pa. Cmwlth. 519, 562 A.2d 402 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant townships and individuals sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania which affirmed the trial court decision granting appellee developer's petition to incorporate a tract of land owned by appellee exclusively. Appellants contended the granting of the petition was an abuse of discretion, and the lower appellate court erred in affirming the order.


OVERVIEW: Appellee developer petitioned to establish a tract of land it owned as a new borough. Pursuant to the Borough Code, the trial court established a Borough Advisory Committee. The committee voted three to two in favor of incorporation, and the court adopted the findings of fact and conclusions of law of the majority report. Appellants townships and individuals sought review, and the lower appellate court affirmed. The court granted allocatur to address whether, under the Borough Code, the trial court abused its discretion. The court affirmed the order holding that appellee's financial backing for its project was contingent, to some extent, on the degree of control held appellee. In addition, the borough was located in two townships. As such, coordination of local zoning ordinances would be required. Under these circumstances, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in granting incorporation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court order granting appellee developer's petition to incorporate a tract of land into a new borough. The court held that there was no abuse of discretion under the circumstances where the land was located in two townships requiring coordination of local zoning ordinances, and financial support was contingent, to some extent, on the degree of control held by appellee.

Blackwell v. State Ethics Comm'n


Nos. 162 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988, 10 M.D. Appeal Docket 1990, 57 E.D. Appeal Docket 1990, 28 M.D. Appeal Docket 1990, 7 E.D. Appeal Docket 1990


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


527 Pa. 172; 589 A.2d 1094; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 85


October 23, 1989, Argued  
March 28, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Re-argued October 23, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from an Order of the Commonwealth Court entered October 25, 1988 at No. 2222 C.D. 1988    Pa. Commw.   , 556 A.2d 988; Appeal from an Order of the Commonwealth Court dated January 22, 1990, entered at No. 2374 C.D. Appeal Docket 1988; Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 46 Misc. Docket 1989; Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court entered on February 1, 1990 at No. 15 Misc. Docket 1990; Appeal from an Order of the Commonwealth Court dated January 8, 1990, entered at No. 2222 C.D. 1988; 130 Pa. Commw. 646, 569 A.2d 378.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Ethics Commission, on application for rehearing sought review of the court's opinion and order quashing the appeal and holding that the Sunset Act, 71 P.S. § 1795.4(4), was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power, and was invalid and void. The court consolidated other cases with the application for reargument, and limited the issue on appeal to the retroactive application of its earlier decision.


OVERVIEW: On application of appellee council members, to quash the review sought by appellant State Ethics Commission, the court held that 71 P. S. § 1795.4(4), of the Sunset Act, was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power, was invalid and void, and quashed the appeal. On appellant's motion for reargument, the court consolidated several other actions wherein the question of retroactivity was also at issue. The court found that the threshold test in deciding whether a new decision might be given prospective application only was whether the decision established a new principle of law, either by overruling clear past precedent or by deciding an issue of first impression whose resolution was not clearly foreshadowed. The court found that its earlier decision did not meet that test, and held that its ruling in the earlier decision would apply to the instant appeal, the instant consolidated appeals, and to all proceedings pending at the time of its earlier, wherein the issue of the constitutionality of the Leadership Committee postponement provision in Section 4(4) of the Sunset Act was timely raised and properly preserved.


OUTCOME: On appellant State Ethics Commission's application for reargument, the court reiterated its holding that the constitutionality of the Leadership Committee postponement provision of the Sunset Act was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power. The court further held that its earlier decision did not specifically declare it to be prospective only, and the court found no compelling reason to so limit its holding.

Muhammad v. Strassburger, McKenna, Messer, Shilobod & Gutnick


No. 5 W.D. Appeal Dkt. 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 541; 587 A.2d 1346; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 49


March 5, 1990, Argued  
March 15, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Reargument Denied May 8, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court at No. 218 Pittsburgh 1987, entered May 13, 1988, reversing the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, Allegheny County, entered January 13, 1987, at No. GD 84-6520 and remanding the case.

 374 Pa. Super. 613, 543 A.2d 1138 (1988).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee parents sought review of an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which held that appellees' claim of legal malpractice against appellant law firm was not barred by collateral estoppel.


OVERVIEW: The court granted allocatur to determine whether appellee parents' claim of legal malpractice, which stemmed from their dissatisfaction with the settlement of their prior medical malpractice action, was barred by the doctrine of collateral estoppel and whether, as a matter of law, appellees had alleged sufficient facts to entitle them to proceed with their action. The court noted that appellees agreed to settle the medical malpractice suit and then changed their minds. The court held that the legal malpractice suit was not barred by the doctrine of collateral estoppel because appellees did not have the opportunity to litigate the question of appellant law firm's alleged negligence in the prior action. The court stated that appellees' action against appellant was barred because they failed to allege sufficient facts. The court stated that the facts that appellees put forth in their complaint, if proved, did not entitle them to relief. The decision of the intermediate appellate court was reversed and the cause dismissed.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's decision that held that appellee parents' claim of legal malpractice was not barred by collateral estoppel was reversed. Additionally, appellees' action was dismissed because the facts as set forth in the complaint, if proven, did not entitle them to relief.

 

181 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jasper


No. 65 E.D. Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 497; 587 A.2d 705; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 48


December 14, 1989, Argued  
March 13, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, Entered at Nos. 1394 and 1396 June Term, 1984 on May 4, 1988.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Because appellant was sentenced to death, the court was compelled to review the judgment of sentence from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which followed conviction of appellant for first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. In imposing the death sentence, the jury determined that the evidence supported two aggravating circumstances and no mitigating circumstances. Appellant raised several issues on review. Among them was a challenge to the trial court's jury instruction with regard to mitigating circumstances. Specifically, appellant asserted that the trial court improperly instructed that a finding of mitigating circumstances had to be unanimous. The court found that the instruction to the verdict and death sentence was proper. However, the court was troubled by the court's response to a question from the jury. The jury asked whether they all had to agree whether a circumstance was true or not true, and the judge answered in the affirmative. Based on this, the court concluded that the jury could have been misled into believing that a unanimous verdict was required in order to conclude a mitigating circumstance existed. It also found that such a perception could have tainted the weighing process and the ultimate outcome. Accordingly, the judgment of sentence was vacated and remanded for a new sentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: Appellant's death sentence was vacated and remanded for a new sentencing hearing because the court concluded that the trial court may have misled the jurors into believing that they had to have a unanimous verdict as to finding mitigating circumstances.

 

182 of 892 DOCUMENTS


Gulnac v. South Butler County School Dist.


No. 69 Western District Appeal Docket 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 483; 587 A.2d 699; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 42


September 25, 1990, Argued  
March 8, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Civil Division - Law at No. 89-012 of 1989, Book 25, Page 142 - In Equity, Dated June 9, 1989.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher's association challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County (Pennsylvania), upon the request for declaratory relief made by appellees, parents and students, finding appellant's limited right to strike was violative of the guaranteed right to education contained in Pa. Const. art. III, § 14.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teachers association sought review of a lower court order, upon the request for declaratory relief by appellees, parents and students, declaring appellant's limited right to strike as violative of Pa. Const. art. III, § 14, which guaranteed the right to an education. On appeal, the lower court ruling was vacated. In support of its ruling, the court held that a declaratory judgment may not be employed to determine rights in anticipation of events that may never occur or for consideration of moot cases or as a medium for the rendition of an advisory opinion that may prove to be purely academic. With respect to the instant case, the court found that once the lower court held that, while appellant's strike was unconstitutional, appellees had no standing to seek relief against them, it became moot whether the teachers had a theoretical right to strike under the state Constitution. Accordingly, the court noted that it was unnecessary to reach the constitutional issue, and by doing so the lower court rendered an impermissible advisory opinion.


OUTCOME: The lower court order was vacated, as the decision became an impermissible advisory opinion once the lower court had determined that appellees did not have the standing to seek the relief that they requested.

Commonwealth v. Edmunds


No. 582 Pittsburgh 1987


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 374; 586 A.2d 887; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 28


March 6, 1990, Submitted  
February 4, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Superior Court dated April 25, 1988 at No. 582 PGH 1987, affirming judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Westmoreland County at No. 1402C 1985, dated January 26, 1987. 373 Pa.Super. 384, 541 A.2d 368.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF SENTENCE REVERSED. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which upheld his arrest and charge for criminal conspiracy, simple possession, possession with intent to deliver, possession with intent to manufacture, and manufacture of a controlled substance. Defendant argued that the search warrant used to conduct the search of his residence was illegal, because it was not based upon probable cause.


OVERVIEW: The police obtained a search warrant to search defendant's residence for drugs that was not based upon probable cause. The police discovered marijuana and arrested defendant for criminal conspiracy, simple possession, possession with intent to deliver, possession with intent to manufacture, and manufacture of a controlled substance. Defendant filed a motion to suppress the evidence obtained during the search, because the warrant was defective. The trial court suppressed the evidence, and the appellate court reversed. Defendant sought further review, and the supreme court held that the warrant was defective because it was not based upon probable cause. The supreme court also determined that even though the police exercised good faith in obtaining the warrant, there was no good faith exception to the warrant requirement under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8. The court stated that to allow a good faith exception would do damage to 200 years of protection under the Constitution to the state citizens. The court reversed the appellate court, and ordered that the evidence obtained should be suppressed at his trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and held that there was no good faith exception to allow evidence admitted into a trial that was obtained on a warrant not issued without probable cause and, therefore, the evidence should be suppressed at defendant's criminal trial.

Pennsylvania Northwestern Distribs. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 89 W.D. Appeal Docket 1989


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 186; 584 A.2d 1372; 1991 Pa. LEXIS 2; 8 A.L.R.5th 970


September 27, 1990, Argued  
January 7, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Substituted January 10, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court at No. 1520 C.D. 1988, dated March 16, 1989, Affirming the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County - Civil Division at No. S.A. 1218 of 1987, dated June 2, 1988, Dismissing the Appeal from the Decision of the Zoning Hearing Board of the Township of Moon; 124 Pa. Commw. 228, 555 A.2d 1368 (1989).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed dismissal of appeal from a zoning board decision upholding the validity of an amortization provision in an ordinance affecting appellant's business.


OVERVIEW: Appellant opened an adult bookstore in appellee township. Thereafter, appellee township enacted an ordinance making appellant's bookstore a nonconforming land use in its then location; the ordinance contained an amortization provision that would have required appellant to cease its business within 90 days. The state supreme court reversed appellee zoning board's determination that the amortization provision was valid. The court held that the amortization and discontinuance of a lawful pre-existing nonconforming land use was per se confiscatory and violative of the Pennsylvania Constitution. Here, there was no evidence that appellant's bookstore violated obscenity laws or was in any other way unlawful, constituted a nuisance, or was abandoned. Thus, appellee township's ordinance was unconstitutional on its face, as it allowed an unconstitutional "taking" of appellant's property without just compensation.


OUTCOME: Order reversed, as amortization provision in appellee township's zoning ordinance was facially unconstitutional where it required the discontinuance of appellant's lawful pre-existing nonconforming land use without providing just compensation.

Commonwealth v. Simon


No. 21 M.D. Misc. Dkt. 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


526 Pa. 69; 584 A.2d 895; 1990 Pa. LEXIS 220


May 2, 1989, Argued  
December 27, 1990, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued May 7, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County dated January 22, 1987 at No. 150 M.D. 1984, transferred by Order of Superior Court entered June 7, 1988 at No. 106 Harrisburg, 1987.     Pa. Super.    ;     A.2d     (19  ). (filed June 7, 1988)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case was before the court on joint application for a stay of remand to review petitioner's claims of error by the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his petition for writ of habeas corpus relative to his criminal contempt sentence. The commonwealth sought review of the order finding 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5947(e) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: While petitioner was imprisoned on drug-related charges, he was granted immunity under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5947(e) to testify before a grand jury. He invoked his privilege against self-incrimination and the trial court found him in civil contempt and sentenced him to prison. When the grand jury went out of existence, the trial court terminated petitioner's confinement and ruled § 5947(e) unconstitutional because it permitted a civil contemnor's imprisonment beyond the existence of the grand jury. Petitioner filed an application for writ of habeas corpus. The trial court dismissed the petition. Acting on a joint application to stay a remand from the appellate court, the court accepted direct review. It ruled that § 5947 was constitutional because petitioner always had the power to purge himself of the contempt, even after the grand jury disbanded. There was no double jeopardy violation when the contempt sentence interrupted petitioner's drug-related sentence because the trial court had inherent authority to suspend the prior sentence for the purpose of enforcing its civil contempt orders. Thus, the court affirmed the order dismissing petitioner's application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing petitioner's application for habeas corpus because the imposition of a civil contempt sentence that suspended his drug-related sentence was not a double jeopardy violation. The court concluded that the statute, which permitted a civil contemnor to be imprisoned beyond the existence of the grand jury, was constitutional because petitioner retained the power to purge himself of the contempt.

Commonwealth v. Basemore

Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


525 Pa. 512; 582 A.2d 861; 1990 Pa. LEXIS 200

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of death sentence imposed under Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 9711, for murder, robbery, and possession of an instrument of crime convictions in favor of appellee state in the Philadelphia Criminal Trial Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of a death sentence imposed pursuant to Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. §. 9711, for a murder conviction, based on numerous urged errors in the sentencing proceeding below. The court affirmed the death penalty in favor of appellee state, concluding the sentence was not a product of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor while findings plainly supported aggravating circumstances of killing while in the perpetration of a felony. The court first rejected the argument that the refusal to grant a continuance denied appellant a fair trial. The court reasoned no abuse of discretion existed in the denial when such continuance to obtain new counsel was requested although defense counsel indicated his investigation was complete and it was an obvious ruse to delay the inevitable. The court determined it was not error to remove appellant from the courtroom when his right to be present was lost through his own intentional disruption and outbursts. The court found prosecutorial argument in favor of death penalty properly included statements about belief of appellant's guilt, quality of mitigating factors, and a reference to lack of sympathy shown the victim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed death sentence imposed upon appellant for a murder conviction in favor of appellee state because sufficient evidence established guilt and sentence was not a product of arbitrary factors or prejudice, but instead was supported by aggravating circumstances and lack of mitigating factors.

Bethenergy Mines v. Workmen's Comp. Appeal Bd. (sadvary)


No. 104 W.D. Appeal Docket 1988


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


524 Pa. 235; 570 A.2d 84; 1990 Pa. LEXIS 62


September 25, 1989, Argued  
February 15, 1990, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Order of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania at Nos. 1826 C.D., 1987, entered on July 6, 1988, reversing the Order entered on July 20, 1987, by the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board at Miscellaneous No. 4365.

 117 Pa. Cmwlth. 465, 543 A.2d 1268 (1988).



DISPOSITION: The Order of the Commonwealth Court is reversed and the Board's Order dismissing Appellee's termination petition is reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order from the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, which reversed a decision of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board that had denied appellee employer's petition for termination of appellant's widow's benefits.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was awarded widow's benefits based upon her husband's work-related death. Shortly thereafter, appellee employer learned that appellant was living with another man, and filed a petition to terminate compensation benefits under the Worker's Compensation Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 562. The Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board (Board) determined that such a finding did not mandate termination of benefits. The lower court reversed the Board, holding that the language of the Act was mandatory and not discretionary and, therefore, once the Board determined that appellant was participating in a meretricious relationship, termination of benefits was required. Appellant challenged the order on appeal. The court concluded that the use of the word "may" granted discretion to the Board. Because the Board was granted discretion, the issue became under what guidelines the discretion could be exercised. The use of economic circumstances as a basis upon which to determine whether benefits should have been continued or terminated was not an arbitrary exercise of the Board's discretion. As such, the Board's exercise of discretion was not arbitrary, and its order was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order overturning the decision of the Workmen's Compensation Appeal Board, holding that the Board had discretion in deciding not to terminate appellant's widow benefits after it was discovered that she was living with an other man. The use of economic circumstances as a basis for making its determination was not an arbitrary exercise of the Board's discretion.

Post-conviction relief denied at Commonwealth v. Williams, 599 Pa. 495, 962 A.2d 609, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 176 (Jan. 22, 2009)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Commonwealth v. Williams, 629 Pa. 533, 105 A.3d 1234, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 3329 (Dec. 15, 2014)
Related proceeding at Commonwealth v. Williams, 634 Pa. 290, 129 A.3d 1199, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 2973 (Dec. 21, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Judgments of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, entered July 1, 1987 at Nos. 2363, 2365 and 2367 of August Term 1984.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of first degree murder, criminal conspiracy, and robbery, and imposed the sentence of death after a finding of two aggravating circumstances and no mitigating circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder, criminal conspiracy, and robbery, and was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and the sentence. The court held there was sufficient evidence presented for the jury to have determined that all elements of the crimes for which appellant was convicted were established beyond a reasonable doubt. There was no answer adduced with respect to whether appellant was a suspect in an unrelated murder, and the issue itself was raised first by appellant. Counsel was not ineffective with respect to the impact of an accomplice's deal on his credibility. The jurors were made aware of appellant's age and whether they did not consider it to be a mitigating circumstance was a decision wholly within their province. Unanimity was not required in establishing mitigating circumstances. The revelation of misdemeanor convictions paled in comparison to the impact upon the jury of the more serious felony convictions. Their revelation did not prejudice appellant's case and counsel was not ineffective for failing to object to them. The sentence of death was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions for first degree murder, criminal conspiracy and robbery were affirmed, as there was sufficient evidence presented for the jury to have determined that all elements of the crimes were established beyond a reasonable doubt. Based on a review of statistical data and the finding of two aggravating and no mitigating circumstances, the sentence of death was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.

Commonwealth v. Sanders


No. 20 EDA 1999


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


1999 PA Super 325; 743 A.2d 970; 1999 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4627


September 20, 1999, Submitted  
December 22, 1999, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the ORDER ENTERED November 19, 1998 In the Court of Common Pleas of MONTGOMERY County CRIMINAL, No. 3564-95. Before NICHOLAS, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged, pro se, the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County which denied, without a hearing, his petition for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9541, et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was denied relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9541, et seq. Appellant alleged that the PCRA's 1995 amendments were null and void because they allegedly comprised legislation which exceeded the scope of the governor's proclamation of designated subjects to be addressed during special session in violation of Pa. Const. art. 3, § 12. The PCRA, which provided a vehicle for a wrongfully convicted person to collaterally challenge his criminal conviction, was certainly not outside the scope of the designated subject matter of the special session on crime, but fell squarely within the subject matter designated by the governor for consideration by the legislature. The lack of a specific request for particular legislation in a governor's call did not render unconstitutional the subsequent enactment of specific legislation where that legislation falls within the ambit of the general subject matter of the call.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed; the court found that the governor's call was for a special session on crime and the 1995 amendments to the Post Conviction Relief Act were squarely within that subject matter; therefore, the court held that the 1995 amendments were not null and void.
